The expert, Frederick C. Cuny, who has worked on emergency relief projects in Biafra, Bosnia, Ethiopia and Somalia, was last heard from in Chechnya nine days ago.   The Soros Foundations hired Cuny to manage a project in Chechnya to help refugees who have fled the Russian offensive against the breakaway region as well as people who have stayed behind in Grozny, the battle-scarred Chechen capital.   Morton Abramowitz, a close friend of Cuny's who is president of the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, said Tuesday that he feared that Cuny was badly wounded or was being detained by one of the sides in the Chechen conflict.   ``There was some talk that he was a prisoner and the Russians had him and the Russians deny it,'' said Abramowitz.   NEW DELHI (NYT) -- Robert E. Rubin's first venture abroad to strengthen alliances through economic diplomacy suffered a surprise reversal of fortunes Tuesday when the treasury secretary found himself constantly crossing paths with another state visitor whom the United States has been struggling to isolate: President Hashemi Rafsanjani of Iran.   Rubin said that until last weekend, when he was attending a meeting of finance ministers in Indonesia, he was unaware that Rafsanjani and his delegation of 100 officials would be arriving just hours ahead of him, for three days of talks that included the possibility of building a of building of an oil pipeline to run between the two countries.   ``Clearly, we would have rescheduled, if we had known in a timely fashion,'' a chagrined Rubin said Tuesday. Shortly before, in what was originally intended as a relationship-building meeting with India's prime minister, P.V. Narasimha Rao, Rubin felt obliged to remind his host in ``fairly clear terms,'' that the United States viewed Iran as an outlaw nation that supports terrorsim.   ABIDJAN, Ivory Coast (NYT) -- At least 62 people, most of them women and children, were killed last week in an unclaimed attack on a village near the Liberian port city of Buchanan, officials of the United Nations Children's Fund said Tuesday.   In the attack, the bloodiest incident in a recent flair-up of violence in Liberia's five-year old civil war, UNICEF officials said that unidentified fighters surrounded the village of Yosi and proceeded to hack its residents to death with machetes.   International relief agency officials based in the Liberian capital, Monrovia, said that no foreigners had yet been able to visit the scene of the massacre, details of which have come from injured survivors who have made their way to Buchanan, 20 miles away.   On Tuesday, a United Nations helicopter ferried four of the most gravely wounded survivors to Monrovia for treatment. Officials of Medecins Sans Frontieres, the French relief agency said that the injured included a badly slashed infant, a pregnant woman who had been repeatedly raped, causing a miscarriage, and a young girl whose head was partially split open by machete blow.   HEBRON, Israeli-Occupied West Bank (NYT) -- By the thousands, Israelis flooded into Hebron Tuesday to worship at the Cave of the Patriarchs shrine, prayers that for many were also a bitter anti-government protest.   This is Passover week, when Israel slows to a crawl and many people skip work. It has been seized by religious and political critics of Prime Minister Yitzhak Rabin as an opportunity to rally large crowds and, they hope, show that his peace policies lack popular support.   On Monday, an estimated 5,000 to 10,000 protestors marched 10 miles from the western edge of the West Bank to the coastal city of Netanya, passing on the way an intersection where 21 Israelis were killed in January by Palestinian suicide bombers. The point was to show how narrow Israeli territory is there, able to be crossed in a few hours on foot and a few minutes by car -- a vulnerable strip, they argued, should a hostile Palestinian state rise in the West Bank.   PARIS (NYT) -- France threatened Tuesday to withdraw its 4,530 peacekeeping troops from Bosnia unless its diplomatic partners and the United Nations could ensure their safety, get the belligerents back to the negotiating table and extend a ceasefire set to expire at the end of this month.   A statement issued by Prime Minister Edouard Balladur after a special cabinet session called in outrage over the killing of two French soldiers in Sarajevo last weekend said that if the safety of the peacekeepers could not be assured, France ``would be forced to decide on a withdrawal of the French soldiers in Bosnia.''   Foreign Minister Alain Juppe flew to New York and asked U.N. Secretary-General Boutros Boutros-Ghali for an emergency session of the U.N. Security Council to discuss what French officials said was a deteriorating security situation in Bosnia.   ``What we are looking for is action by the international community to avert the necessity of a withdrawal, not an excuse to leave,'' one of Juppe's aides said.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950418.0202 type=story > <HEADLINE> PAKISTANI YOUTH, 12, CITED FOR RIGHTS WORK, MURDERED </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-YOUTH-SHOT-BOS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199504.gz"

In fact, she and her family were sheltered in the embassy after the 1956 Hungarian revolution.   ``My parents were Hungarian journalists on the run, and they spent part of my childhood in prison on false charges of being CIA agents,'' she said. ``After they got out, we lived in the embassy for a few weeks.''   That's not her only attachment to Budapest. ``We did part of our courting there,'' she said. ``Richard was ambassador to Germany when we started this grand romance, and we made our first trip together there.''   Holbrooke served in that post briefly last year.   Ms. Marton was recently divorced from Jennings after a two-year separation. They have two children, as does Holbrooke.   ``Richard asked me to marry him a year ago,'' she said. ``I don't want to gush, but I didn't realize such happiness was possible at our ripe age. We have a very grown-up relationship.''   She is 45, and he is 53.   NEW YORK -- Talk about stress in the workplace. On Saturday, the last day of the Metropolitan Opera season, Placido Domingo will sing the title role in Wagner's ``Parsifal,'' and in the evening he will conduct Puccini's ``Madama Butterfly.''   ``It was coincidence,'' Domingo said Wednesday. And when the scheduling people at the Met realized it, they offered to replace him as conductor with Samuel Cristler.   Had the order of the two operas been reversed, he might have accepted the offer. ``I couldn't conduct in the afternoon and sing in the evening because conducting takes a lot of your breathing and you need all the support for singing,'' the tenor said. ``This will be unique.''   It certainly will. ``Parsifal,'' which begins at noon, won't end until about 5:30, and ``Madama Butterfy'' starts at 8 and lasts until 11.   Domingo sounded relaxed, though. ``I will have two and a half hours between,'' he said, ``during which I will rest a bit, put myself to sleep, I hope, and then have a light meal.''  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950419.0486 type=story > <HEADLINE> TERROR BLAST SHOCKS A NORMALLY QUIET TOWN </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> OKLAHOMA CITY, Okla. DAVID LETTERMAN ``LATE SHOW'' TOP TEN  Bob Dole Complaints About Hollywood  10. Why pay seven bucks to see boozing and adultery when you can watch Democrats for free?  9. No Academy Award for grouchiest senator.  8. Not enough Fonzie.  7. In ``Don Juan Demarco,'' Marlon Brando shows way too much cleavage.  6. Those ``Baywatch'' babes never seem to get it on with conservative old coots.  5. Why couldn't Gump shake hands with a senate majority leader instead of President Kennedy?  4. ``Casper the Friendly Ghost''? More like ``Casper the Bare-Ass Naked Ghost''!  3. Gets sick to stomach watching Letterman at the concession stand (videotape of Letterman buttering his face)  2. ``Dumb and Dumber'' was clearly propaganda for the Clinton/Gore ticket.  1. That creepy Richard Simmons guy (Simmons busts into theater).  BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Overheard on our bus: ``It's beginning to look as if O.J. stands for 0 jurors.''  Another way to think of Judge Ito is as a baseball manager who already has used up his bench and bullpen by only the 6th inning.  If there's a Simpson mistrial because of too few jurors, officials next time should know better. They should pick 12 jurors with no human flaws.  Maybe this big debate about sleaze coming from the entertainment industry could have been avoided had the place been named Hollywoodn't.  ``I agree with a lot of what Senator Dole said'' about Hollywood, Mr. Clinton says. This is what you call crypartisanism.  Gerald Ford says Bob Dole will get the GOP presidential nomination ``unless he makes a mistake or stumbles.'' Similarly, the federal government will balance the budget _ unless it spends more than it takes in.  ``I'm not happy with everything that's happening in Bosnia,'' says Mr. Clinton. Nor, we hope, in Washington.  ``Homeownership,'' the president says, ``encourages savings and investment.'' Especially if you're the plumber or electrician paid by the homeowner.  ``Vice President Gore is the most involved vice president in the history of the country,'' says Mr. Clinton. Isn't that like calling someone the most successful Cubs' manager in 50 years?  Bill Clinton says he intends to run again in 1996 with Al Gore. It's unclear, however, whether Gore will wait for a better offer.  CITY STREETS  Child, age 3-ish, while inserting an assortment of colored pegs into a pegboard: Mommy, tell me about making decisions.  Mother: Well, you make decisions about what you are going to do or how you are going to do something. Right now you are deciding where to put the pegs. You are making decisions.  Child: No, I'm not making decisions. I'm making a horse.  Place: Belasco Theater, West 44th Street.  Time: Opening night of ``Hamlet.'' Following a two-hour first act, my friend Amy and I head for the ladies' room. ``You know,'' I say, ``it would be nice if they could all get married and live happily ever after, but they all die at the end.''  Suddenly, a voice raised in disbelief and disappointment comes from behind me. ``They all die at the end?!''  I spin around, heart pounding, mind racing: This woman spent $65, sat through two hours and I've ruined the play for her. Maybe I should issue an apology. Maybe I should deny everything, in hopes of keeping her dangling. Instead, I turn away. I turn to face her again. This time she's smiling mischievously.  Note from a friend:  Cocktail talk, between fistfuls of peanuts at the Algonquin.  The topic at one table was charge accounts. ``You won't believe what happened last week,'' Woman No. 1 said. `` `Pay per view' refused to connect me with a movie because they said I owed too much.''  ``How humiliating!'' her companion sympathized.  ``Tell me!'' Woman No. 1 said. ``Even Bloomingdale's never did that.''  ``I'll drink to that,'' Woman No. 2 said.  And so they did.  Christopher Gammill is strolling on East 64th Street on a warm spring evening when a limo pulls up. A father and his 3- or 4-year-old daughter emerge.  A young woman and her golden retriever are passing by at the same time.  ``Don't bite the dog!'' the father says. ``Don't bite the dog!''  ``Oh, don't worry,'' the retriever's owner says. ``My dog won't bite her.''  ``You don't understand,'' Dad says, pointing to his daughter. ``She likes to bite dogs.''  To be filed under ``W,'' as in ``Who says New Yorkers aren't friendly?''  The place is Sheridan Square, where the fluffiest dog bounces along, trying to keep up with its owner. From the other direction stomps a body-builder type, leather jacket, tattoos, the works. As they pass one another, fluffy stops to sneeze, then ambles on. Mr. Muscle looks back over his broad shoulders. ``God bless,'' he says in a rumbling voice.  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!!  Offer not available in any store! (Or any other news service!) Story will move in ``w'' Washington category.  BRIGHT STUDENTS-PERILS  UNDATED _ A Harvard University pre-med student hacks her roommate to death before hanging herself. A Fort Worth, Texas, honor student is accused of masterminding a fake bomb scare that resembled some aspects of the deadly explosion in Oklahoma. How tragic, the public murmurs. How sad. But because these episodes involved talented, high-achieving students, another collective thought comes to mind: What could they possibly have been thinking? By PATRICIA RODRIGUEZ. Story has moved in both ``l'' lifestyle and ``a'' general news categories.  COUSCOUS  UNDATED _ It sounds like a name you might call your cat when you chuck her under the chin; it looks like a grain of rice that hasn't yet reached puberty. No wonder supermarkets don't know where to stock couscous. Identified with Morocco and the rest of North Africa, couscous (pronounced KOOS-koos) is really nothing more than another pasta shape. In the United States, sales have grown 20 percent to 25 percent a year since 1989. Some recipes involving couscous are included. Story has moved in two takes in ``d'' food and dining category. But that can change with a little work. By ART CHAPMAN. Story has moved in ``d'' food and dining category. By BEVERLY BUNDY and JUNE NAYLOR RODRIGUEZ. Story has moved in two takes in ``d'' food and dining category.  ROCK-QUEENSRYCHE  UNDATED _ It's no question that the members of Seattle's Queensryche had earned a vacation at the end of the world tour that followed their 1990 CD ``Empire.'' The band had put out five CDs in seven years. It had pulled itself up from the ranks of opening for AC/DC and Def Leppard to arena-headliner status with the success of the ``Operation: Mindcrime'' CD and the success of ``Empire'' via the hits ``Silent Lucidity'' and ``Jet City Woman.'' Now the band is back after a period of self-reflection. By DAVE FERMAN. Story has moved in ``e'' entertainment category. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950607.0404 type=other > <DATELINE>  (BC-BONG-BULL-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> No. 323 for May 24-25  The Burned-Out Newspapercreatures Guild endorses U.S. Sen. and presidential candidate Phil Gramm for his courageous stand against the Hollywood liberal moguls' insidious grip on soft porn movie financing, and this is BONG Bull No. 323!  BULLETIN FROM THE WORLD PRESS CONSPIRACY COUNCIL. The Internet places where journalists gather have become tarbabies of La Brea proportion, pulling in loonies who post messages such as ``Peter Jennings phone me!'' And the worldwide press conspiracy is a prominent whipping boy, now that every stool-sitter in every cowboy bar has heard from a guy down at the yard's barber about the microchips in the money and the black helicopters, and how the press is setting up the big takeover.  The proof of a conspiracy is that no one has mentioned the real microchips -- the ones in the beer labels, so secret that they're not even being denied.  But the press has some serious defenders, even if they seem to be giggling as they post. A typical message: ``As we like to say in our shop, we don't have enough TIME to create a conspiracy, and we sure don't have the manpower to perpetuate it. And if we did, breaking news from O.J. would probably kill it, and we sure as hell can't take any time from sports and weather.''  HEADS WILL ROLL. Michele Dula Baum, now checking for clues at The Chattanooga Times, (MDulaBaol.com) reports, ``Back in the early '80s, when Gene Autry remarried at an elderly age, a night editor at The Johnstown (Pa.) Tribune-Democrat mused that `He's Back In the Saddle Again' but rejected it as too risque. The next morning, The Pittsburgh Post-Gazette ran with it. Alas, those opportunities for double entendre are far too rare to pass up.''  -- George Hesselberg (hessstatejournal.madison.com) of the Wisconsin State Journal advises, ``There were three of us left in the old Wisconsin State Journal newsroom the night Nixon fired Archibald Cox. We were finishing our Old Milwaukees ($1.10 a six pack from the Shamrock across from the Capitol) when the word came.  ``Steve Lovejoy, myself and Richard Schwarz (we all co-authored a book, Cocktails at Molotovs, consisting of newsroom bulletin board offerings, never printed, of course). Anyway Lovejoy grabs a sheet of copy paper and types out a top line: Nixon Sacks Cox, rolls it up and stuffs it into the tube and sends it on its way. Of course, deadlines were past and no one was around, but we had a great time imagining we could get it into the paper.  ``We were all late night cop reporters trained by the legendary June Dieckmann (This is Junie, baby), who had a Pabst IV in the hospital when she got her throat cancer cut out. She carried a two-shot derringer and a sixpack in her purse, never wore stockings (in Wisconsin) and cried when she put up the office Christmas tree alone every year.''  Yes, well, a fine story, George, and illustrative of your well-earned title of Most Dangerous Columnist in Wisconsin. And, um, how much is Old Milwaukee this week?  CORRECTION. Time to get serious.  Some members of the Pendulum mailing list on the Internet are sensitive to possible stigma that might be attached to those with a mental disorder (in this case, bipolar disorder), and quickly responded to BONG Bull 322's item about cartoonist Scott Adams on computer users being the sex symbols of the 90's. They point out that Adams' article was a repost to Pendulum, not written originally for it. (A list member's further repost reached BONG with the original source data lopped off.)  As we told Pendulum, it is BONG's policy to credit material from other sources as best we can, partly because when BONGers don't get information on how to sign onto other keen sources, flame mail arrives. We logged onto the list for a few days last week, received lots of e- mail from it, and found its members to be amusing and personable individuals. As for Pendulum, Ed Arnold (eraucar.edu) invites contacts from people who seek other people who care. Arnold's non- computer links: P.O. Box 3000, Boulder, Colo. 80307-3000, phone (303) 497-1253, fax 497-1804.  WHY A BURNED-OUT WRETCH IS THE PARTNER OF YOUR DREAMS. With or without computer skills, the great undiscovered romance opportunity of our age is that cynical, crusty pro chewing down the last half of the last cigar in newspaperdom, right there in the lonely corner of your newsroom. Half-close your eyes (it blurs the cigar) and see him or her in a new light:  -- Burnouts are always available, once you get past their initial suspicion that you're warming them up for a new project team.  -- Burnouts know things. Useful things, like how to drop in on refreshments-provided staff meetings before the cold cuts are gone, but after the worst of the blather. They can find other people who don't want to be found. They employ exciting new methods to make office machines work after the dayside experts have gone home.  -- Burnouts' nose for news is hyperdeveloped, though often in different ways from their cop shop days. Like, they mysteriously know which glitched candy machine in the building is giving away Snickers bars 14 for a quarter, even if the machine is three floors underground and even the publisher doesn't go there.  -- When the burnout looks good to you, it's proof that you finally have overcome the meddlesome influences of childhood mentors, parents and clergy.  -- Other people are not rivals for the attentions of burnouts.  BONG GETS INK. Editor &AMP; Publisher goes for big streetrack sales in its May 13 issue with the Chief Copyboy's article, ``What I Would Study if I Went Back to College.'' It was an essay question for a doctoral program, written before anybody said you had to be a graduate before you can get into a post-grad program. So it went to E&AMP;P. Waste not, want not.  CATALOG SHOPPE. The famous BONG Pejorative Posters, listing more than 120 newspaper names, except the names -- Slimes, Crimes, Mess, Pest -- aren't what the publisher wishes the staff and public would call them; 11-by-17, $3 each postpaid to any address in North America. Collectors on other continents or planets should add shekels or samoleons accordingly. Also:  -- Tie tacks/lapel pins, sawn from old typewriters, $12.50. Earrings (specify clip or pierced), $12.50 a pair. Specify preferred characters. These caused great excitement at the South Bend (Ind.) chapter of SPJ's fundraising auction last week.  -- The Chagrin Falls Commercial Scimitar Foreign Correspondent press card, personalized (specify name), $1.50.  -- This year only, the Stare-Fixing Deskplate, any combination of up to 13 letters on fine wood; most people want their names there but hey, it's (ital) your (unital) desk. $39.50.  Order all from BONG Chief Copyboy Charley Stough, 2517 Rugby Road, Dayton, Ohio 45406.  COMIX SECTION. The Further Adventures of Herman ``Speed'' Graphic, Ace Photographer for the Chagrin Falls Commercial Scimitar, and his Faithful Companion, Typo the Wonder Pig.  PANEL ONE: A slightly harried Typo greets his pal as Speed returns from the coffee desk wearing his trenchcoat, a deathbed gift from an ancient mystic wire service executive editor on a fog-shrouded eastern island, and declares, ``Ah, Boss, you're back! The results of the new Employee Sensitivity Survey are about to be posted!''  Speed queries, ``Employee sensi -- what?''  PANEL TWO: Typo explains, ``Right, Boss! You were upstairs discussing your South Bend trip with Features Editor Hyperba Lee so I filled it out for you and signed your name! Look, the secret results show that photographers see themselves as lovable, friendly, courteous and hard-working! And so do sports writers!''  Speed demands, ``Friendly, courteous -- sports writers?''  PANEL THREE: Glancing at the door, Typo reads on, ``In fact, every department saw itself as lovable, friendly, courteous and hard-working except the copy desk, which added contumelious and lubricious about themselves, jejune in reference to the editorial writers!''  Speed queries, ``Je-what? Editorial write -- ?''  PANEL FOUR: Sliding the print washer across the doorway, Typo explicates, ``Indeed, Boss! In fact, the results become really interesting when departments describe other departments, especially your spine-tingling analysis of the whole organization chart, with candid photos from the press convention! Positively ingenious! Prodigal, they'll sing of you! It's a best-seller!''  Speed requests, ``My analysis of -- ?''  PANEL FIVE: Stepping onto the window ledge and reaching for the downspout, Typo declaims, ``Historic, Boss! You'll be legendary among personnel consultants! And take your time with your doughnut -- it'll be at least six minutes before they get the battering ram in place, and nothing will happen to the negatives, I have them all safely with me!''  Never far from the wistful vista of the escape route or the comforting protection of an alias, BONG Chief Copyboy Charley Stough, Dayton Daily News, 45 S. Ludlow St., Dayton, Ohio 45401 rates NYTNS colleagues Top Drawer! E-mail copyboydmapub.dma.org. Phone (513) 225-2445 after 3 p.m. eastern. The misdemeanor charge carries up a $1,000 fine and up to one year in jail. By Jaxon Van Derbeken. DAVID LETTERMAN ``LATE SHOW'' TOP TEN  Bob Dole Complaints About Hollywood  10. Why pay seven bucks to see boozing and adultery when you can watch Democrats for free?  9. No Academy Award for grouchiest senator.  8. Not enough Fonzie.  7. In ``Don Juan Demarco,'' Marlon Brando shows way too much cleavage.  6. Those ``Baywatch'' babes never seem to get it on with conservative old coots.  5. Why couldn't Gump shake hands with a senate majority leader instead of President Kennedy?  4. ``Casper the Friendly Ghost''? More like ``Casper the Bare-Ass Naked Ghost''!  3. Gets sick to stomach watching Letterman at the concession stand (videotape of Letterman buttering his face)  2. ``Dumb and Dumber'' was clearly propaganda for the Clinton/Gore ticket.  1. That creepy Richard Simmons guy (Simmons busts into theater).  BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Overheard on our bus: ``It's beginning to look as if O.J. stands for 0 jurors.''  Another way to think of Judge Ito is as a baseball manager who already has used up his bench and bullpen by only the 6th inning.  If there's a Simpson mistrial because of too few jurors, officials next time should know better. They should pick 12 jurors with no human flaws.  Maybe this big debate about sleaze coming from the entertainment industry could have been avoided had the place been named Hollywoodn't.  ``I agree with a lot of what Senator Dole said'' about Hollywood, Mr. Clinton says. This is what you call crypartisanism.  Gerald Ford says Bob Dole will get the GOP presidential nomination ``unless he makes a mistake or stumbles.'' Similarly, the federal government will balance the budget _ unless it spends more than it takes in.  ``I'm not happy with everything that's happening in Bosnia,'' says Mr. Clinton. Nor, we hope, in Washington.  ``Homeownership,'' the president says, ``encourages savings and investment.'' Especially if you're the plumber or electrician paid by the homeowner.  ``Vice President Gore is the most involved vice president in the history of the country,'' says Mr. Clinton. Isn't that like calling someone the most successful Cubs' manager in 50 years?  Bill Clinton says he intends to run again in 1996 with Al Gore. It's unclear, however, whether Gore will wait for a better offer.  CITY STREETS  Child, age 3-ish, while inserting an assortment of colored pegs into a pegboard: Mommy, tell me about making decisions.  Mother: Well, you make decisions about what you are going to do or how you are going to do something. Right now you are deciding where to put the pegs. You are making decisions.  Child: No, I'm not making decisions. I'm making a horse.  Place: Belasco Theater, West 44th Street.  Time: Opening night of ``Hamlet.'' Following a two-hour first act, my friend Amy and I head for the ladies' room. ``You know,'' I say, ``it would be nice if they could all get married and live happily ever after, but they all die at the end.''  Suddenly, a voice raised in disbelief and disappointment comes from behind me. ``They all die at the end?!''  I spin around, heart pounding, mind racing: This woman spent $65, sat through two hours and I've ruined the play for her. Maybe I should issue an apology. Maybe I should deny everything, in hopes of keeping her dangling. Instead, I turn away. I turn to face her again. This time she's smiling mischievously.  Note from a friend:  Cocktail talk, between fistfuls of peanuts at the Algonquin.  The topic at one table was charge accounts. ``You won't believe what happened last week,'' Woman No. 1 said. `` `Pay per view' refused to connect me with a movie because they said I owed too much.''  ``How humiliating!'' her companion sympathized.  ``Tell me!'' Woman No. 1 said. ``Even Bloomingdale's never did that.''  ``I'll drink to that,'' Woman No. 2 said.  And so they did.  Christopher Gammill is strolling on East 64th Street on a warm spring evening when a limo pulls up. A father and his 3- or 4-year-old daughter emerge.  A young woman and her golden retriever are passing by at the same time.  ``Don't bite the dog!'' the father says. ``Don't bite the dog!''  ``Oh, don't worry,'' the retriever's owner says. ``My dog won't bite her.''  ``You don't understand,'' Dad says, pointing to his daughter. ``She likes to bite dogs.''  To be filed under ``W,'' as in ``Who says New Yorkers aren't friendly?''  The place is Sheridan Square, where the fluffiest dog bounces along, trying to keep up with its owner. From the other direction stomps a body-builder type, leather jacket, tattoos, the works. As they pass one another, fluffy stops to sneeze, then ambles on. Mr. Muscle looks back over his broad shoulders. ``God bless,'' he says in a rumbling voice.  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!!  Offer not available in any store! (Or any other news service!) BE A NEWSROOM COMMITTEE CHAIRMAN, OR BE A NEWSROOM STAIN. It's your choice. Here are the, you should pardon the hackneyed expression, paradigms:  -- As part of their act, newsroom committee chairmen are always walking fast and looking worried. But newsroom stains are always there when important visitors come around.  -- Newsroom committee chairmen get a line to put in their resumes.  Newsroom stains are legendary and require no resume.  -- Nobody snickers in the face of the newsroom committee chairman.  Nobody snickers behind the back of a newsroom stain.  -- Rookies never look at newsroom committee chairmen and ask ``What's the story behind that one?'' even if there is a story. Rookies do ask about stains even if there isn't. For this reason, stains' legends grow.  -- Newsroom committee chairmen are only notable when they're in the chair. Hmm. So are stains. Well, OK, this one's a wash.  -- Newsroom committee chairmen's pictures can easily be taken off the wall, their fame and rank subject to whim. Stains require repainting.  -- Newsroom committee chairmen have to stand in line for staff cars. Stains are already in them.  -- Nobody complains when a newsroom stain wastes space and doesn't solve any problems. Newsroom committee chairmen have to post the minutes.  -- Newsroom committee chairmen must live up to their mothers' inflated images of them. Newsroom stains don't.  -- If somebody wants to remove a newsroom committee chairman, it's much messier than removing a stain.  NEWS OF THE NEWS FROM THE NET. The BONG spinoff column, exploring the dialogue found in the thousands of Usenet special-interest groups, this week finds some Internet ``personals'' areas. And what do you know, if you're looking for love in soc.men and soc.women (group names), forget it. That's where you go to get yelled at by members of the opposite sex who think you're selfish, too fussy about appearances, ignorant of personal hygiene, insensitive, overbearing and can't manage a zipper.  And so's your meddlesome mother.  But actually there's hope for a relationship if you're tall, unmarried and want to pose for watercolor. There is also great wisdom available for those who want to steal books. It's all in this week's News From the Net by Charles Stough, weekly from the New York Times News Service, the most noble wire service of them all.  Alas, there are no plans to distribute NFTN to BONG's on-line members. But if they call up and harass their local managing editors into running the column, lo! magic will happen.  MORE ABOUT THE CITY OF PUNDITS. Faithful readers will recall the BONG crusade to have all the world's columnists, editorial writers and cartoonists emigrate to isolated Sanderson, Texas, rocketing their output to distant newsrooms by electronic means and establishing a majority in the little burg that can set its own rules of behavior, assuming they want any.  Comes now this unrehearsed, unsolicited report from Gene Charleton (e-charleton (at) tamu.edu) of the Texas A&AMP;M Office of University Relations :  ``All the good things your correspondent reports about Terrell County and Sanderson, Texas, are true. But you don't know what you're asking when you call for volunteers to confirm them. Sanderson is a long way from everywhere, y'all. Even in Texas. Except maybe Dryden and the Gage Hotel in Marathon.  ``Sanderson is idyllic because crime and urban blight just don't know where to find it. I mean, Sanderson's idea of crime prevention used to be the sign on the convenience store door that read (approximately) `Stickup artists please remove ski masks and unload pistols before entering.'' ' Is that great or what? A town that's like being at sea, only the waves don't make your stomach want to get out and paddle home alone.  But there's more. David P. Garland (garland (at) hti.net), a photographer in Austin, Texas, amplifies:  ``In 1987, the Amtrak Sunset Limited was stranded at the Sanderson station for several hours because of a freight train derailment ahead.  The people of Sanderson got together and put on a barbecue for the passengers on the train. The event made the news wires and is still talked about today on the railroad.'' Plenty of office space for rent there, he said. And if you just go sit on the tracks for a few hours, they feed you!  DISPATCH FROM THE DOWN UNDER BUREAU. Peter Ford of the Seven TV Network in Sydney, Australia (psford (at) ozemail.com.au) relates, ``G'day colleagues: I've been reading editions of BONG for the past two months and it's terrific. We haven't seen pioneering journalism like this since Clements left Hannibal with his newfangled typewriter.  ``I'm with the Seven Television Network (we number them down here instead of giving them letters such as CBS and NBC) in Sydney, and like the States, newsrooms here have their own special brand of mental deviation.  ``A Colleague With Another Network (and Who Shall Remain Nameless Outside These Shores) has made a name for himself with his unwittingly colourful leads over the years. Two gems recently mined:  `` `The headless body was found lying face down in the mud outside...' and `The coroner found that the victim had been beaten savagely around the head with a two by four, when a simple tap would have sufficed...' Colleagues will swear on a case of Fosters this is absolutely true. Yours in journalism, Peter Ford.'' Ford admits to 14 years in the States as a news anchor with CNN and NBC in Miami and Washington, D.C. ``I came back here because I missed the Outback deserts, and have reacquired an Aussie accent -- like the Midwest in geography and twang, except you talk through your nose -- it makes drinking beer easier. Laptops make everything possible now: about five weeks ago, I filed a story to Space News in Northern Virginia from a remote high security rocket base in the heart of our central desert, via CompuServe.''  SEARCH RESULTS. The BONG web page (http://www.au.com/bong/) is now searchable by keyword. Mike Linksvayer (mlinksva (at) netcom.com), chairman of the Electronic Media and Punch Recipe Research Committee, advises that browser-equipped members will discover that in the 50 key editions posted, Simpson is mentioned in six, Chronicle in eight, and Dole and Clinton in two each.  Of more than 4,000 requests to the web page, Linksvayer reports, ``One issue, 326, has been read over 700 times. The reason? It contains the word `pedophilia,' and lots of people are searching the net for documents containing that word.'' Well, yes, that was the edition in which we said the Internet was not all pornography and pedophilia. Maybe we were wrong. But even more mysterious, why do so many people take such an avid interest in bicycle riding?  THE UNMENTIONABLES. Evan Roth (rothe (at) washpost.com) whines, ``OK, Charley, you've been hawking your new column almost every BONG Bull lately. Would you dare do the same for your competition? Probably not.'' What? Mention CyberSurfing, LAT-WP's weekly attempt? Nah, no way.  QUERY FROM THE OUTPOST. Hal Davis (hdavis (at) ljextra.com) at National Law Journal, keeper of the most astonishing and eclectic tickler file in journalism, forwards these notes:  -- Herb Caen reported that Oakland Tribune editor David Burgin choked on an apple in the newsroom and was saved by a Heimlich hugger.  It was Burgin's third such close call (at S.F. Examiner and Orlando Sentinel), and that's only as of April. ``We think it's the only sex he gets,'' an Examiner editor told Caen.  -- UPI's financial desk asked Don Mullen, NYC local desk, if it was possible to get repetitive stress syndrome of the brain. Mullen wired back, ``YES IT IS. HOWEVER, IT DOES NOT APPLY TO ANYONE WHO HAS WORKED IN THIS BUREAU FOR OVER A YEAR. WHAT YOU HAVE IS CURRENTLY INCURABLE AND IS CALLED SIEGE DWEEBISM. IT IS IN THE PROCESS OF BEING DEFINED. ONE OF THE BASIC TESTS TO FIND IF YOU ARE ON THE WAY TO BECOME A SIEGE DWEEB IS TRYING TO STARE INTO YOUR OWN EYES EACH MORNING IN THE BATHROOM MIRROR.  IF YOU CAN LAST LONGER THAN 25 SEDCONDS THERE IS SOME HOPE. IF YOUR FACE BEGINS TO CRUMPLE BEFORE 20 SECONDS YOU ARE PROBABLY A CHRONIC S.D.  MULLEN''  -- The unofficial Unipresser newsletter ``--95--'' published by Dick Harnett, 555 Laurel Ave 322, San Mateo CA 94401 holds ``the most memorable UPI message was probably `UPSTICK JOB ASSWISE.'' ' A query as to its origin is guaranteed to lead to at least a couple of beers' worth of anecdote. Harnett's phone is (415) 344-6419; fax (415) 342-4674.  COMIX SECTION. The Further Adventures of Herman ``Speed'' Graphic, Ace Photographer for the Chagrin Falls Commercial Scimitar, and his Faithful Companion, Typo the Wonder Pig.  PANEL ONE: Speed and Typo hide in the motor pool grease pit and review their performance at the staff meeting. Typo calculates, ``Right, Boss! We got there on time, didn't dominate the doughnut plate, kept our shoes on and admired Features Editor Hyperba Lee's power scarf!''  PANEL TWO: Speed wonders, ``I've seen people promoted to assistant managing editor for less, Typo! What went wrong?''  PANEL THREE: Typo dislodges a crumpled copy of the agenda from Speed's trenchcoat, a deathbed gift from an ancient mystic wire service executive editor on a fog-shrouded eastern island, and continues, ``You said two nice things about any other department, Boss! Your excuses about the Sunday 5-Star were just as good as everybody else's! You laughed just as loud at TV's story from City Hall that we didn't have and you cried just as hard at the readership survey results!'' Speed ventures, ``Well ... could it have been my sample for a great new column idea?''  PANEL FOUR: Framed by the hydraulic hoist, the Deft Duo ponder in silhouette.  PANEL FIVE: Typo proposes, ``Well, let's not think about showing up the committee favorites, Boss! I think I see where the mechanics keep their lunch pails, so at least we won't starve tonight!''  First and foremost in all things old hat, BONG Chief Copyboy Charley Stough, Dayton Daily News, 45 S. Ludlow St., Dayton, Ohio 45401 tips a fedora to NYTNS skimmers worldwide! You Could Do Lunch What Happens When the Bar Association Gives a Symposium and Nobody Comes (bl) c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  Every day for months, Dan Lowenstein, the chief of staff for New York state Sen. Catherine M. Abate, had been asking Wendi Paster, the New York City chief of staff for Assemblyman Richard N. Gottfried, ``When are we ever going to get married?''  The couple, who had been engaged since February, had decided to marry in September and, in fact, got a marriage license in August.  But, they explained Friday, they could never carve out the time to set a date or find a place.  ``We spent a lot of time working for our bosses and volunteering for political events, so we had trouble putting together a party for ourselves,'' Lowenstein said.  Then, on the subway to work Wednesday, Ms. Paster announced, ``Today.''  ``We know enough judges,'' she said, ``and I figured we could call one and be married on our lunch hour.''  At 1 p.m., they were wed by Judge Rosalyn H. Richter of Manhattan Criminal Court in her chambers, with Gottfried and his wife, Louise (who lent Ms. Paster her wedding ring), as witnesses. After lunch at a Vietnamese restaurant, they went back to work.  Lowenstein was responsible for a fund-raising event that evening for Sen. Abate.  A honeymoon this weekend? Not a chance. Lowenstein's mother, Ellie Lowenstein, is running for the planning and zoning board in Westport, Conn., so the newlyweds are campaigning there for her.  ``We're still going to have a wedding with a rabbi and a big party,'' Ms. Paster vowed. ``But things are gearing up for the legislative session in Albany, plus there are the presidential primaries, the congressional and state races . . . ''  c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  It was one of those moments of truth on the lecture circuit in New York: big-name speakers facing a sparse audience. Most of the panel at a symposium on how movies and television depict the legal profession ignored the fact that fewer than 150 of the 500 seats were filled in the Great Hall of the New York City Bar Association.  But in his speech at the concluding session Thursday, Edwin Sherin, an executive producer of ``Law and Order,'' the hit television series, confronted the situation head-on.  ``I expected standing-room-only crowds,'' Sherin said of the bar association's program, which included the actor Sam Waterston; Justice Edwin Torres of the state Supreme Court, who is also a crime novelist, and Thomas H. Baer, a movie producer and lawyer.  ``This is somewhat of a shock,'' Sherin said. ``I would have thought that all those cars doubled-parked outside were people coming in this evening.''  ``I was a performer for many years,'' he continued, ``so when I look out and see such a sparse and, I hope, active group, I get somewhat disturbed, and I am reminded of the significant failures that I participated in during my career in the theater, television and film.  ``But I am very happy to say that `Law and Order' is not one of them.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950922.0490 type=story > <HEADLINE> AS O.J. One woman who did so recounts a financial tale of horror how her irresponsible son racked up some $3,000 in debt before the card was turned into confetti. By DAN LYNCH.  MATH-CONCEPTS (San Francisco) _ If you asked a typical kid what number squared plus three equaled the 12 days of Christmas, he'd probably tell you that you were an ignorant fool. Silicon chips have made such questions moot, he'd say. The sad truth is that fewer than one in three San Francisco grade schoolers demonstrated a full command of math concepts on recent state tests. By SCOTT WINOKUR. Times News Service for Wednesday, March 13:  KOREAN-GROCERS (New York) _ For years, the Jungs, who moved the family to New York from Seoul, South Korea, in 1983, have worked to send their daughters to good schools and, they hope, better lives. For the children, it has worked out; one is at Harvard, one at Bryan Mawr. But things aren't looking so good for their parents. There is a glut of small groceries in the city now, and it looks as if they will lose their business. By Elaine Louie. (With ART)  PROTEIN-DIET (Undated) _ Frank McCullough, a book editor in Manhattan who has lost 60 pounds in the last year, is relishing the response of people who haven't seen her for a while (``Is that really you, Frank?''). Friends of Janet Pope, a decorative artist in Baltimore, shake their heads in awe at her weight loss _ 56 pounds in 13 months _ accomplished, like Ms. McCullough's, by cutting down on carbohydrates and concentrating on protein. Buoyed by these testimonials filling my ear, driven by curiosity and burdened with 14 surplus pounds, I joined the protein packers for five weeks, in direct opposition to my normal, deeply committed, plant-based complex-carbohydrate life. Ultimately, I lost weight, gained new admiration for protein, kept my respect for complex carbohydrates and began to question some of the very tenets of dietary dogma. By Suzanne Hamlin. (With ART)  REPUBLICAN-GRILL (Washington) _ The hot place in Washington for the Republican power elite and those who would court them. With plenty of steaks, cigars and martinis. By Marian Burros. (With ART)  BROWNING-FOOD (Undated) _ Turning the heat way up produces some great food; with browning, the technique is on top of the stove. (With Recipes.)  STANDARD FEATURES  NATION'S-TABLE (Undated) _ Fayetteville, Ark.: James at the Mill, where the French-trained chef, Miles James, is celebrating down-home cooking with what he calls Ozark Plateau Cuisine. Housed in a recently historic landmark.  Denver: Amid the rush of microbreweries opening in connection with the new baseball stadium Coors Field, Enoteca LoDo, the ``wine library,'' opens. It's a wine bar that's housed in one of Denver's historic buildings. The centerpiece of the 134-seat wine bar and restaurant is the Winekeeper, a 40-spigot contraption that serves wine by the glass.  Chicago: 15 years after the opening of Ambria, one of Chicago's top high-end restaurants, Gabino Sotelino, a co-owner and former chef, is back in the kitchen. (With ART)  PLAIN&AMP;SIMPLE (Undated) _ Weekly recipe. By Marian Burros.  WINETALK (Undated) _ Every year a magazine called Wine &AMP; Spirits surveys top restaurants on what wine customers are drinking. This produces a list of the top 50 restaurant wines and a lot of other stuff on wine service, on what customers don't like, good wine lists, etc. The restaurants are the top picks in 18 regional Zagat guides. By Frank J. Prial.  WINE-TASTINGS (Undated) _ Capsule review of an interesting, moderately priced wine. INTERNATIONAL _ (Moved in ``i'' category)  MIDEAST-SUMMIT (Sharm El Sheik, Egypt) _ In one sense, the more than two dozen world leaders gathering at this Sinai resort Wednesday to prop up the tottering Middle East peace effort will have succeeded just by showing up, according to some of the sponsors. By Douglas Jehl.  (ART ADV: Locator map has been sent to NYT graphics clients.)  MIDEAST-INTEL (Washington) _ When the television lights go out after the anti-terrorism summit meeting in Egypt on Wednesday, the real work will begin in the dark: an international effort to form a counter-terrorism alliance involving the espionage services of half a dozen nations. By Tim Weiner.  ISRAEL-TERROR (Jerusalem) _ The wave of suicide attacks has unsettled daily life here, permeating moments of routine with the fear of another terrorist strike, perhaps timed to coincide with the summit conference to open in Egypt on Wednesday. By Joel Greenberg.  JAPAN-UNWED-MOTHERS (Tokyo) _ Only 1.1 percent of births in Japan are to unwed mothers, compared with 30.1 percent in the United States. In America, the proportion has soared in recent years, but in Japan, it has tumbled. Social stigma and economic hardships, delayed sexual activity, easy access to abortion are factors. By Sheryl WuDunn.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT15) of Keiko Nakada playing with her son has been sent to NYT photo clients.)  KENYA-ELEPHANTS (Kimana Tikondo Group Ranch, Kenya) _ Elephants may be great for tourism, but not for Masai vegetable gardens. A Foreign Journal. By James C. McKinley Jr.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT5) to illustrate story has been sent to NYT photo clients. Graphic to illustrate story has been sent to NYT graphics clients.)  NATIONAL _ GENERAL (Moved in ``a'' category)  CA-LIBRARY-GAYS (San Francisco) _ There is a corner for almost everybody in the new San Francisco Public Library, nearing completion in the Civic Center area, but the most unusual one can be found on the third floor, where the James C. Hormel Gay and Lesbian Center is housed. By David W. Dunlop.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT10) showing Charley Brown and Mark Evans with the mural they designed for the Gay and Lesbian Center has been sent to NYT photo clients.)  ILLINOIS-PATRONAGE (Springfield, Ill.) _ Republican politicians in Illinois, a state long known for its political dealings, are now rising to the defense of political patronage. By Dirk Johnson.  SC-NUKE-WASTE (Aiken, S.C.) _ After years of delay, U.S. finally begins safe (it says) storage of nuclear waste from bomb-making facilities. By Matthew L. Wald.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT9) to illustrate story has been sent to NYT photo clients.)  KREMLINOLOGY-EDUC (Undated) _ With breathtaking suddenness in 1991, the cadre of academicians popularly known as Kremlinologists _ pampered and privileged by years of federal, foundation and academic patronage _ found themselves outmoded. The Soviet Union had collapsed, the Cold War was over and they, despite their vaunted scholarship, linguistic proficiency and esoteric reporting skills, had utterly failed to forsee one of the turning points of the 20th century. By William Honan.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT14) to illustrate story has been sent to NYT photo clients.)  ASTHMA-STORM-HEALTH (Undated) _ Doctors have traced an epidemic of asthma attacks in England to a severe thunderstorm. By Lawrence K. Altman.  ALCOHOL-TOLERANCE (Undated) _ A study finds that men who at age 20 had a low response to alcohol _ they needed to drink more than most people in order to feel any effects _ were at increased risk of becoming alcoholics within a decade, regardless of their drinking habits early on. Bob Dole, the Republican front-runner, faces a pair of prospects no one has ever faced before. By R.W. Apple Jr.  WASHINGTON _ (Moved in ``w'' category)  CONGRESS-UNIONS _ Having applied to itself the rules that govern the rest of the nation, Congress watches as unions like the Teamsters and the Fraternal Order of Police try to organize its employees. By Eric Schmitt.  BUSINESS DAY _ (Moved in ``f'' category)  DRUG-PRICE-BATTLE _ In a powerful sign of the shifting power balance, drug manufacturers and managed care companies across the country are squaring off in a battle over pricing and influence in the enormous $65 billion market for prescription drugs. By Milt Freudenheim.  (ART ADV: Two graphics on HMOs and drug pricing have been sent to NYT graphics clients.)  FED-WEALTH (Washington) _ The rich appear to have gotten richer in the early years of this decade, according to federal statistics released Tuesday, but this time they did it at the expense of the super-rich. By Richard W. Stevenson.  (ART ADV: Graphic on Fed's wealth survey has been sent to NYT graphics clients.)  ANN-TAYLOR-MARKETPLACE _ Whether a retailer sells brooms or bras, it is dangerous to try and sell too many of them. Just ask Ann Taylor. By Jennifer Steinhauer.  STYLE (Moved in ``l'' category)  PARIS-FASHION-I (Paris) _ Anyone looking for a signal that fall 1996 will be a strong Paris season had one every second with the thundering tribal beat at the show of Dirk Bikkembergs here Monday night. With a thumping bass so loud and powerful it reset the heartbeat, the show began a round of collections that will last nine days, with 82 houses on the official schedule. By Amy M. Spindler.  (ART ADV: Photos [NYT11,12) to illustrate story have been sent to NYT photo clients.)  PARIS-FASHION-II (Paris) _ Issey Miyake, in the collection he showed on Tuesday, faced down the fact that the fashion consumer is not the same person she was 27 years ago, when he started designing in Paris. By contrast, on the Dior runway it seemed as if time had stood still. By Constance C.R. White.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT13) to illustrate story has been sent to NYT photo clients.) For the children, it has worked out; one is at Harvard, one at Bryan Mawr. But things aren't looking so good for their parents. There is a glut of small groceries in the city now, and it looks as if they will lose their business. By Elaine Louie.  PROTEIN-DIET (Undated) _ Frank McCullough, a book editor in Manhattan who has lost 60 pounds in the last year, is relishing the response of people who haven't seen her for a while (``Is that really you, Frank?''). Friends of Janet Pope, a decorative artist in Baltimore, shake their heads in awe at her weight loss _ 56 pounds in 13 months _ accomplished, like Ms. McCullough's, by cutting down on carbohydrates and concentrating on protein. Buoyed by these testimonials filling my ear, driven by curiosity and burdened with 14 surplus pounds, I joined the protein packers for five weeks, in direct opposition to my normal, deeply committed, plant-based complex-carbohydrate life. Ultimately, I lost weight, gained new admiration for protein, kept my respect for complex carbohydrates and began to question some of the very tenets of dietary dogma. By Suzanne Hamlin.  (ART ADV: Photo [NYT7) to illustrate story has been sent to NYT photo clients.) (NYT20) WASHINGTON -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HOLOCAUST-REDCROSS, 12-18 -- Georges Willemin, Red Cross archivist, left, and Walter Reich, director of the United States Holocaust museum, sign a cooperative agreement. The International Committee of the Red Cross released copies of its World War II files Wednesday, some of which provided verification that it knew of the persecution of Jews in Nazi concentration camps but felt powerless to speak out. (Stephen Crowley/New York Times Photo)  (NYT19) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FOREIGN-EXCHANGE-CLR, 12-18 -- In the rough-and-tumble world of foreign exchange trading, where $1.2 trillion changes hands every day, Matthew Lifson fought his way through a string of small banks all the way to the big show - a job at Chase Manhattan Bank and the presidency of Forex USA, the industry association. ``I can see the market continuing to consolidate,'' he says. ``But if these new markets arise, there will be need for traders.'' (Steve Berman/New York Times Photo)  (NYT18) TAMPA, Fla. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FBN-GIANTS-CLR, 12-18 -- Bill Parcells, in his finale as Giants coach, is carried off the field after a Super Bowl XXV victory over Buffalo in January 1991. When he brings the Patriots into the Meadowlands Saturday, Parcells will coach against his former team for the first time. ``I can't say there won't be some fond memories,'' he says. ``But what it means to me is that if we win the game and get a first-round bye, that can be a big advantage in the playoffs.'' (Vic DeLucia/New York Times Photo)  (NYT17) KEY BISCAYNE, Fla. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PHILANTHROPY-CLR, 12-18 -- The Aaron Diamond Foundation, led by Irene Diamond, plans to dissolve on Dec. 31, having given all of its $200 million to finance ambitious projects, including a Manhattan laboratory at the forefront of AIDS research. By spending huge chunks of their assets each year until the principal is gone, such high-profile benefactors are hoping that they will have a greater impact on societal problems. (Andrew Itkoff/New York Times Photo)  (NYT16) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- CHESS-WOMEN-DECLINE-CLR, 12-18 -- Young participants in the Fifth Annual U.S. Chessathon, held at the Intrepid Sea-Air-Space Museum on Saturday. Even as chess has grown in popularity and gained a foothold in American classrooms, the sport remains mystifyingly male-dominated and oddly segregated. Of the world's roughly 450 grandmasters, six are women; only one, Judith Polgar of Hungary, is ranked among the world's top 100 players. (Monica Almeida/New York Times Photo)  (NYT15) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HARLEM-BOYS-CHOIR-CLR, 12-18 -- Members of the Boys Choir of Harlem rehearse at their school. Despite its high profile, the 28-year-old choir has frequently had to scramble for money, and now, according to its director, Walter Turnbull, it faces a financial crisis that threatens to drastically diminish its voice. ``People don't realize that we are about much more than singing,'' he says. ``What we're really doing is trying to save lives.'' (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT14) QUEPOS, Costa Rica -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- COSTARICA-ECOTOURISM-CLR, 12-18 -- Tourists watch a pair of white-faced monkeys perched on trash bins at Manuel Antonio National Park. Nurtured by the Costa Rican government and foreign conservationists as an ecologically pristine alternative, this Pacific Coast reserve appears to have become a victim of its own success: Unsanitary conditions have ended camping within the park, and the hotels here are now dumping their sewage into the sea. (Juan Carlos Ulate/New York Times Photo)  (NYT13) HOLLYWOOD -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- BACALL-MOVIES, 12-18 -- After some lean years, Lauren Bacall recently made three films. She's ready for more. ``I mean, I've had a lot of dry years,'' she says. ``So these pictures will, I hope, bring attention to the fact that I am around, breathing, walking and talking at the same time.'' (Nathaniel Welch/New York Times Photo)  (NYT12) PLANO, Ill. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PALUMBO-HOUSE-COLLECTOR-2-CLR, 12-18 -- Mad for modern: Lady Palumbo, wife of Lord Peter Palumbo, at Mies' Farnsworth House in Plano, which she calls ``the most romantic house in the world.'' The Farnsworth House is the steel-and-glass jewel of Lord Palumbo's multinational architectural crown. (Todd Buchanan/New York Times Photo)  (NYT11) UNDATED -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PALUMBO-HOUSE-COLLECTOR-1-CLR, 12-18 -- ``I'm a sucker for columns,'' says Richard Hampton Jenrette, the soft-spoken Wall Street financier who recently retired as chairman of the Equitable Life Assurance Company, lounging on the grounds of his plantation, called ``Millford.'' A North Carolina native, Jenrette has restored seven houses of Tara-like grandeur. (Fred R. Conrad/New York Times Photo)  (NYT10) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HENRYVI-THEATER-REVIEW-CLR, 12-18 -- Jan Leslie Harding as Joan of Arc in Part 1 of ``Henry VI,'' directed by Karin Coonrod. Ms. Harding portrays the historical icon not as the diabolical witch of the text but as a strong, simple yokel drunk on delusions. Using a very accomplished ensemble of 10 actors to play at least several dozen parts, Ms. Coonrod has telescoped and rearranged the text to sustain a galloping pace and to keep bewilderment at a minimum. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT9) LOS ANGELES -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- CALIF-MONDRIAN-HOTEL, 12-18 -- The Mondrian hotel, Hollywood's newest hot spot, is a magnificent design for dubious living: a smart place for a time when smart people are finding it very hard to merge. Johnny Carter, 9, takes a reclusive break on the terrace of the hotel. (Susan Werner/New York Times Photo)  (NYT8) BLOOMINGTON, Minn. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- MINN-MALL-CLR, 12-18 -- Mom touch: Va-Lesha Beeks, left, and Lynn Jones, right, help Brandy Madsen, a security officer, keep teen-agers in line at the Mall of America. Such action is at the heart of the Mighty Moms program, which was innaugurated here last spring to help keep order at the 4.2 million sqaure-foot city of 520 stores and a 7-acre amusement park. (Steve Woit/New York Times Photo)  (NYT7) CONCORD, Mass. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PRIMARY-CARE-BOS-CLR, 12-18 -- Dr. Virginia Latham is one of thousands of primary care physicians on the front lines of a managed-care system that keeps health costs down by limiting access to services and reducing reimbursements. As part of an Emerson Hospital internal medicine practice, Dr. Latham checks Lynn Devitt's ears in her Concord office. (Joanne Rathe/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT6) LEXINGTON, Mass.-- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PROFILE-NYE-BOS-CLR, 12-18 -- Joseph Nye, who is involved with ex-CIA director James Woolsey in a special task force on nuclear terrorism, with one of the three chickens he raises in his backyard chicken coop. Nye, who has been dean of Harvard's Kennedy School of Government for the past year, is the author of an important Defense Department strategy, known as the ``Nye Initiative,'' which deals with Japan and the rising power of China. Cannon and Sarasohn, who've both been at Centerline barely a year, are trying to turn the company around. ``The problem is a lack of a shared vision in the company with a passion behind it,'' explains Sarasohn. (David R. Ryan/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT4) UNDATED -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HOME-IMPROVEMENT, 12-18 -- If you've never papered a room, it's best to start with a simple room like a hallway or small bedroom. As your skills develop, you can tackle more complicated jobs like the kitchen or bathroom. Make sure that the walls are properly prepared and primed (this was covered in last week's Home column). If the ceiling, molding or trim needs to be repainted, do it before wallpapering. (Edward R. Lipinski/New York Times Photo) *LITE  (NYT3) BOSTON -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FOOD-COOKBOOKS-BOS-CLR. 12-18 -- This year's crop of cookbooks has an intelligent voice. Authors and publishers have finally come around to understanding certain essential things about home cooks: They have time constraints; they cook for people who worry about their hearts and hips; and not everyone can shop at five specialty stores before every simple meal. These are just a few of the cookbooks on shelves this holiday season. (John Tlumacki/Boston Globe Photo) *LITE  (NYT2) SHERBORN, Mass. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- SHOBIZ-COW-BOS-CLR, 12-18 -- Emily the Cow with Meg Randa at the Peace Abbey in the Vegetarian Resource Center. Emily's story made international headlines last December after she escaped from a slaughterhouse by jumping over a 5-foot holding gate and disappearing into the woods. First Look Pictures, a motion picture distribution company, has purchased the rights to the Emily saga and has begun work on the screenplay. (Bill Greene/Boston Globe Photo) *LITE  (NYT1) SEATTLE -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- LAVA-LAMPS-SPI-CLR, 12-18 -- Lava lamps, ridiculed almost to death and relegated to storage, landfills or thrift shops all over America, have found a new audience. The glass-enclosed brightly colored oil, which is heated by a light bulb until it melts and starts forming weird shapes, is casting a special glow on this holiday season. (NYT20) WASHINGTON -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HOLOCAUST-REDCROSS, 12-18 -- Georges Willemin, Red Cross archivist, left, and Walter Reich, director of the United States Holocaust museum, sign a cooperative agreement. The International Committee of the Red Cross released copies of its World War II files Wednesday, some of which provided verification that it knew of the persecution of Jews in Nazi concentration camps but felt powerless to speak out. (Stephen Crowley/New York Times Photo)  (NYT19) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FOREIGN-EXCHANGE-CLR, 12-18 -- In the rough-and-tumble world of foreign exchange trading, where $1.2 trillion changes hands every day, Matthew Lifson fought his way through a string of small banks all the way to the big show - a job at Chase Manhattan Bank and the presidency of Forex USA, the industry association. ``I can see the market continuing to consolidate,'' he says. ``But if these new markets arise, there will be need for traders.'' (Steve Berman/New York Times Photo)  (NYT18) TAMPA, Fla. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FBN-GIANTS-CLR, 12-18 -- Bill Parcells, in his finale as Giants coach, is carried off the field after a Super Bowl XXV victory over Buffalo in January 1991. When he brings the Patriots into the Meadowlands Saturday, Parcells will coach against his former team for the first time. ``I can't say there won't be some fond memories,'' he says. ``But what it means to me is that if we win the game and get a first-round bye, that can be a big advantage in the playoffs.'' (Vic DeLucia/New York Times Photo)  (NYT17) KEY BISCAYNE, Fla. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PHILANTHROPY-CLR, 12-18 -- The Aaron Diamond Foundation, led by Irene Diamond, plans to dissolve on Dec. 31, having given all of its $200 million to finance ambitious projects, including a Manhattan laboratory at the forefront of AIDS research. By spending huge chunks of their assets each year until the principal is gone, such high-profile benefactors are hoping that they will have a greater impact on societal problems. (Andrew Itkoff/New York Times Photo)  (NYT16) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- CHESS-WOMEN-DECLINE-CLR, 12-18 -- Young participants in the Fifth Annual U.S. Chessathon, held at the Intrepid Sea-Air-Space Museum on Saturday. Even as chess has grown in popularity and gained a foothold in American classrooms, the sport remains mystifyingly male-dominated and oddly segregated. Of the world's roughly 450 grandmasters, six are women; only one, Judith Polgar of Hungary, is ranked among the world's top 100 players. (Monica Almeida/New York Times Photo)  (NYT15) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HARLEM-BOYS-CHOIR-CLR, 12-18 -- Members of the Boys Choir of Harlem rehearse at their school. Despite its high profile, the 28-year-old choir has frequently had to scramble for money, and now, according to its director, Walter Turnbull, it faces a financial crisis that threatens to drastically diminish its voice. ``People don't realize that we are about much more than singing,'' he says. ``What we're really doing is trying to save lives.'' (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT14) QUEPOS, Costa Rica -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- COSTARICA-ECOTOURISM-CLR, 12-18 -- Tourists watch a pair of white-faced monkeys perched on trash bins at Manuel Antonio National Park. Nurtured by the Costa Rican government and foreign conservationists as an ecologically pristine alternative, this Pacific Coast reserve appears to have become a victim of its own success: Unsanitary conditions have ended camping within the park, and the hotels here are now dumping their sewage into the sea. (Juan Carlos Ulate/New York Times Photo)  (NYT13) HOLLYWOOD -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- BACALL-MOVIES, 12-18 -- After some lean years, Lauren Bacall recently made three films. She's ready for more. ``I mean, I've had a lot of dry years,'' she says. ``So these pictures will, I hope, bring attention to the fact that I am around, breathing, walking and talking at the same time.'' (Nathaniel Welch/New York Times Photo)  (NYT12) PLANO, Ill. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PALUMBO-HOUSE-COLLECTOR-2-CLR, 12-18 -- Mad for modern: Lady Palumbo, wife of Lord Peter Palumbo, at Mies' Farnsworth House in Plano, which she calls ``the most romantic house in the world.'' The Farnsworth House is the steel-and-glass jewel of Lord Palumbo's multinational architectural crown. (Todd Buchanan/New York Times Photo)  (NYT11) UNDATED -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PALUMBO-HOUSE-COLLECTOR-1-CLR, 12-18 -- ``I'm a sucker for columns,'' says Richard Hampton Jenrette, the soft-spoken Wall Street financier who recently retired as chairman of the Equitable Life Assurance Company, lounging on the grounds of his plantation, called ``Millford.'' A North Carolina native, Jenrette has restored seven houses of Tara-like grandeur. (Fred R. Conrad/New York Times Photo)  (NYT10) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HENRYVI-THEATER-REVIEW-CLR, 12-18 -- Jan Leslie Harding as Joan of Arc in Part 1 of ``Henry VI,'' directed by Karin Coonrod. Ms. Harding portrays the historical icon not as the diabolical witch of the text but as a strong, simple yokel drunk on delusions. Using a very accomplished ensemble of 10 actors to play at least several dozen parts, Ms. Coonrod has telescoped and rearranged the text to sustain a galloping pace and to keep bewilderment at a minimum. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT9) LOS ANGELES -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- CALIF-MONDRIAN-HOTEL, 12-18 -- The Mondrian hotel, Hollywood's newest hot spot, is a magnificent design for dubious living: a smart place for a time when smart people are finding it very hard to merge. Johnny Carter, 9, takes a reclusive break on the terrace of the hotel. (Susan Werner/New York Times Photo)  (NYT8) BLOOMINGTON, Minn. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- MINN-MALL-CLR, 12-18 -- Mom touch: Va-Lesha Beeks, left, and Lynn Jones, right, help Brandy Madsen, a security officer, keep teen-agers in line at the Mall of America. Such action is at the heart of the Mighty Moms program, which was innaugurated here last spring to help keep order at the 4.2 million sqaure-foot city of 520 stores and a 7-acre amusement park. (NYT20) WASHINGTON -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HOLOCAUST-REDCROSS, 12-18 -- Georges Willemin, Red Cross archivist, left, and Walter Reich, director of the United States Holocaust museum, sign a cooperative agreement. The International Committee of the Red Cross released copies of its World War II files Wednesday, some of which provided verification that it knew of the persecution of Jews in Nazi concentration camps but felt powerless to speak out. (Stephen Crowley/New York Times Photo)  (NYT19) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FOREIGN-EXCHANGE-CLR, 12-18 -- In the rough-and-tumble world of foreign exchange trading, where $1.2 trillion changes hands every day, Matthew Lifson fought his way through a string of small banks all the way to the big show - a job at Chase Manhattan Bank and the presidency of Forex USA, the industry association. ``I can see the market continuing to consolidate,'' he says. ``But if these new markets arise, there will be need for traders.'' (Steve Berman/New York Times Photo)  (NYT18) TAMPA, Fla. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- FBN-GIANTS-CLR, 12-18 -- Bill Parcells, in his finale as Giants coach, is carried off the field after a Super Bowl XXV victory over Buffalo in January 1991. When he brings the Patriots into the Meadowlands Saturday, Parcells will coach against his former team for the first time. ``I can't say there won't be some fond memories,'' he says. ``But what it means to me is that if we win the game and get a first-round bye, that can be a big advantage in the playoffs.'' (Vic DeLucia/New York Times Photo)  (NYT17) KEY BISCAYNE, Fla. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PHILANTHROPY-CLR, 12-18 -- The Aaron Diamond Foundation, led by Irene Diamond, plans to dissolve on Dec. 31, having given all of its $200 million to finance ambitious projects, including a Manhattan laboratory at the forefront of AIDS research. By spending huge chunks of their assets each year until the principal is gone, such high-profile benefactors are hoping that they will have a greater impact on societal problems. (Andrew Itkoff/New York Times Photo)  (NYT16) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- CHESS-WOMEN-DECLINE-CLR, 12-18 -- Young participants in the Fifth Annual U.S. Chessathon, held at the Intrepid Sea-Air-Space Museum on Saturday. Even as chess has grown in popularity and gained a foothold in American classrooms, the sport remains mystifyingly male-dominated and oddly segregated. Of the world's roughly 450 grandmasters, six are women; only one, Judith Polgar of Hungary, is ranked among the world's top 100 players. (Monica Almeida/New York Times Photo)  (NYT15) NEW YORK -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- HARLEM-BOYS-CHOIR-CLR, 12-18 -- Members of the Boys Choir of Harlem rehearse at their school. Despite its high profile, the 28-year-old choir has frequently had to scramble for money, and now, according to its director, Walter Turnbull, it faces a financial crisis that threatens to drastically diminish its voice. ``People don't realize that we are about much more than singing,'' he says. ``What we're really doing is trying to save lives.'' (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT14) QUEPOS, Costa Rica -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- COSTARICA-ECOTOURISM-CLR, 12-18 -- Tourists watch a pair of white-faced monkeys perched on trash bins at Manuel Antonio National Park. Nurtured by the Costa Rican government and foreign conservationists as an ecologically pristine alternative, this Pacific Coast reserve appears to have become a victim of its own success: Unsanitary conditions have ended camping within the park, and the hotels here are now dumping their sewage into the sea. (Juan Carlos Ulate/New York Times Photo)  (NYT13) HOLLYWOOD -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- BACALL-MOVIES, 12-18 -- After some lean years, Lauren Bacall recently made three films. She's ready for more. ``I mean, I've had a lot of dry years,'' she says. ``So these pictures will, I hope, bring attention to the fact that I am around, breathing, walking and talking at the same time.'' (Nathaniel Welch/New York Times Photo)  (NYT12) PLANO, Ill. -- Dec. 18, 1996 -- PALUMBO-HOUSE-COLLECTOR-2-CLR, 12-18 -- Mad for modern: Lady Palumbo, wife of Lord Peter Palumbo, at Mies' Farnsworth House in Plano, which she calls ``the most romantic house in the world.'' The Farnsworth House is the steel-and-glass jewel of Lord Palumbo's multinational architectural crown. Using a very accomplished ensemble of 10 actors to play at least several dozen parts, Ms. Coonrod has telescoped and rearranged the text to sustain a galloping pace and to keep bewilderment at a minimum.
