The articles, which moved within the last week, are from The New York Times (NYT), Boston Globe (BOS), Cox News Service (COX), Hearst News Service (HNS), San Francisco Chronicle (SFCHRON), Los Angeles Daily News (LADN) and Fort Worth Star-Telegram (TEX).  Photos, where indicated, have been sent to NYT photo clients. Nonsubscribers can make individual purchase by calling 212-556-4204.  For retransmissions of these stories, please call 212-556-1927.  GENERAL  EXCLUSIVE-CARTER-RELIGION-NYT (Plains, Ga.) _ As he strolled into the simple sanctuary of Maranatha Baptist Church, the Bible-toting Sunday school teacher was greeted by instant applause and an explosion of flashbulbs. ``Good morning everybody,'' Jimmy Carter said with a smile. ``Good morning,'' his 250 pupils responded in unison. By Kevin Sacks. W/photo. Moved 12-15.  PROFILE-NYE-BOS (Lexington, Mass.) _ Joseph S. Nye Jr. knows about weapons systems not even Tom Clancy can tell you about. What he says about U.S. policy in Asia is carefully tracked in Tokyo and Beijing. He is extremely close friends with the retired chief of the CIA. And he raises chickens. By John Yemma. W/photo. Moved 12-18.  CONDIT-PROFILE-SFCHRON (Washington) _ The son of an Oklahoma Baptist preacher, U.S. Rep. Gary Condit is a walking contradiction. At 48, he still radiates clean-cut boyishness _ but he's nobody's patsy. By Carolyn Lochhead. Moved 12-18.  STEPHANOPOULOS-LEGACY-NYT (Washington) _ It is a tiny warren, cluttered with coffee cups and crumpled papers, Greek icons, a bottle or two of expensive wine and row upon row of books. But in a world where proximity is power, the White House office of George Stephanopoulos, right next to the Oval Office, is the ultimate address. By Todd Purdum. W/photo. Moved 12-13.  PROFILE-SCHROEDER-BOS (Washington) _ Rep. Patricia Schroeder 56 blazed a trail 24 years ago in an institution that was hostile to women, particularly to brash and independent ones. ``It was a jock-ocracy,'' recalled Schroeder, whose wit made her a legend on Capitol Hill. ``People just forget what it was like.'' By Cliff Black. Moved 12-16.  MOVIES  ACTOR-RUSH-SPI (Seattle) _ Australian actor Geoffrey Rush is a late bloomer. After 23 years on the stage, the 45-year-old Rush has suddenly become a major movie star Down Under with ``Children of the Revolution,'' ``On Our Selection'' and ``Shine.'' By Paula Nechak. Moved 12-18.  MOTHER-FILM-$ADV22-NYT (Undated) _ There is a moment in the trailer for ``Mother,'' the new comedy by the director Albert Brooks opening Christmas Day, that says it all. Portraying one side of a telephone conversation about the movie, Brooks brags about landing the ``legendary Debbie Reynolds'' for the title role. A long pause ensues. A frown creases his face. ``No,'' he adds with a huff. ``She's (italics)not(end italics) dead.'' By Frank Bruni. Moved 12-18 for release 12-22.  FLYNT-LARRY-SPI (Seattle) _ Smut merchant Larry Flynt takes a puff off a Cuban ``cee-gar'' and mumbles in a thick, raspy voice that could be coming from a back-alley derelict. The topic is his new respectability. By M.L. Lyke. W/photo. Moved 12-16.  COX-MOVIE-LADN (Los Angeles) _ Courtney Cox wants you to know that she's not obsessive. She's really interested in obsession in a kind of passionate, analytical way, but hardly like Monica Geller, the needling neat freak she plays so convincingly on the hit sitcom ``Friends.'' By Bob Strauss. Moved 12-16.  COUNT-ENGLISHPATIENT-NYT (Budapest) _ The character compassionately depicted in the film ``The English Patient'' as a desert explorer with enigmatic origins is based on a real-life Hungarian who served German military intelligence in World War II and then apparently spied for the Soviet Union. Through an adventure-strewn life that traversed wildly different nations and cultures through two World Wars, the collapse of the Austro-Hungarian empire and the takeover of his country by the Communists, the tall, slender, slightly stooped, multilingual count often seemed many things to many people. By Jane Perlez.Moved 12-16.  MOVIES-MILLER-BOS (Undated) _ Looking more like a robust uncle than an age-bent grandfather, 81-year-old playwright Arthur Miller smiles seraphically. He's smiling for several reasons _ all connected to the Friday release of the film version of his now-classic witch-hunt drama, ``The Crucible.'' By Jay Carr.Moved 12-16.  FILM-ANGELS-SFCHRON (Undated) _ Angels are coming to a theater near you, and they're anything but cherubic. John Travolta is the heavenly messenger in ``Michael,'' coming out on Christmas Day, while Denzel Washington appears as an angel named Dudley in ``The Preacher's Wife,'' which opened this weekend. Both tell the story of all-too-human angels who come down to earth and into the plots of romantic comedies. Both of these sexy saviors wear trench coats, but only Travolta has wings. By Don Lattin.Moved 12-12.  FILM-CLOONEY-SFCHRON (Beverly Hills) _ George Clooney arrived in Hollywood in the early 80s when the group of young actors known as the Brat Pack was on the rise. He read for the same parts as Judd Nelson and some of the others, but they were always hired instead. Then, in 1990, Clooney was almost cast as the hunky cowboy in ``Thelma &AMP; Louise.'' When Brad Pitt got the role, which would make him a star, ``I was pretty upset,'' Clooney admits. By Ruthe Stein.Moved 12-12.  BURTON-MOVIE-LADN (Los Angeles) _ He grew up on Evergreen Street in Burbank and discovered a playground for his imagination at the nearby Evergreen Cemetery, where he could take refuge from the world in the somber, spooky silences. In his new movie _ ``Mars Attacks!'' _ Tim Burton, now 38, indulges his childhood passion for B-grade space-invader movies with a goofy, extravagantly realized send-up of the genre. By Amy Dawes. Moved 12-12.  BACALL-MOVIES-NYT (Hollywood) _ She's 72, still glamorous and feisty, and looking for work. ``Suddenly I've finished three pictures, can you imagine?'' said Lauren Bacall with a soft laugh. ``But now I'm unemployed. I mean, I've had a lot of dry years. So these pictures will, I hope, bring attention to the fact that I am around, breathing, walking and talking at the same time.'' She shrugged. ``Whoever said it would be easy?'' By Bernard Weinraub. W/photo. Moved 12-18.  SHOBIZ-COW-BOS (Sherborn, Mass.) _ Last year, she was the Cow that Came for Christmas. This year, she is the Holstein Headed for Hollywood. Emily the Cow, a 3-year-old, black-and-white heifer who lives at the Peace Abbey here, is perhaps the most beloved bovine this side of Bombay. Emily's true and touching tale is about to become, well, a moooovie. By Doreen Iudica Vigue. W/photo. Moved 12-17.  BEAVISGUY-AZR (Los Angeles) _ The dumber you are, the cooler you are. That's a concept Mike Judge has sold to the world's teenagers. When you meet Judge, you can't help but think that this is what his creations _ ``Beavis and Butt-head'' _ will look like if they ever grow up. Judge, who writes, draws and does the voices for both teenage masters of the vulgar, is balding, sallow and a solemn eye-averter. By Bob Fenster. Moved 12-18.  STARTREK-FRAKES-PROFILE-NYT (New York) _ Even out of his Starfleet uniform, Jonathan Frakes, who has made a lucrative career out of playing the clear-eyed executive officer of the starship Enterprise in the ``Star Trek'' spinoffs, looks like a man in orbit. And no wonder: After acting for more than 20 years, many of them during the 1980s playing what he calls ``villains of the week'' in television dramas like ``Quincy'' and ``Barnaby Jones,'' he has just made his debut as a film director in ``Star Trek: First Contact.'' By Michel Marriott. W/photo. Moved 12-17.  THEATER  ALLISON-JANNEY-THEATER-NYT (Undated) _ Noel Coward's ``Present Laughter'' may be the chain-smoking-est play on Broadway, and though Allison Janney maintains she had quit a half-pack-a-day habit, it looks like the actress has slid backward a bit. On Nov. 18, the day before her 36th birthday, Ms. Janney made her Broadway debut in the revival of the 1939 Coward comedy. She plays Liz, the former but not ex-wife of Garry Essendine (Frank Langella), a London matinee idol who heads a frenetic, neurotic show-biz family. By Jean Nathan. Moved 12-12.  ALBEE-COX (Undated) _ Dismissed by critics who pronounced his career dead throughout the 1980s, playwright Edward Albee is getting the last laugh. By Hap Erstein.Moved 12-19.  TELEVISION  OPRAH-CELEBRITYHAWKING-TEX (Undated) _ The marketing power of Oprah Winfrey's television show, now in its 11th season, has always proven potent, but it appears that Winfrey clearly understands how to use The Force. By Ken Parish Perkins. Moved 12-12.  MUSIC  SAMPLING-RAP (New York) _ The New York City apartment is filled with bookshelves, but most of them don't hold books. Instead, they overflow with albums _ classical over here, jazz over there, and lots of soul and rock-and-roll in between. A parakeet named Adri flies around the living room offering guests, as the bird's owner puts it, ``sweet gangsta kisses.'' Most always there's the steady thump thump of hip-hop blasting in the background. You have entered the world of Hope Carr. By Charisse Jones. Moved 12-17.  CROWS-MUSIC-LADN (Los Angeles) _ Along with the voice and dark-night-of-the-soul lyrics of singer Adam Duritz on hits such as ``Mr. Jones,'' ``Round Here'' and ``Rain King,'' David Bryson's ringing electric guitar is Counting Crows the band's most distinctive calling card. By Fred Shuster. Moved 12-18.  BOOKS  FITZGERALD-CENTENNIAL (Undated) _ F. Scott Fitzgerald's centennial year should not end without a final celebration, especially now, when fiction has lost its pride of place as the form all serious writers must measure themselves by. He is one of those rare artists with a cultural radar system that is constantly picking up sensations, responses and fresh thoughts: still at the center of everything that is modern or postmodern. By Margo Jefferson. Moved 12-16. COUNT-ENGLISHPATIENT-NYT (Budapest) _ The character compassionately depicted in the film ``The English Patient'' as a desert explorer with enigmatic origins is based on a real-life Hungarian who served German military intelligence in World War II and then apparently spied for the Soviet Union. Through an adventure-strewn life that traversed wildly different nations and cultures through two World Wars, the collapse of the Austro-Hungarian empire and the takeover of his country by the Communists, the tall, slender, slightly stooped, multilingual count often seemed many things to many people. By Jane Perlez.Moved 12-16.  MOVIES-MILLER-BOS (Undated) _ Looking more like a robust uncle than an age-bent grandfather, 81-year-old playwright Arthur Miller smiles seraphically. He's smiling for several reasons _ all connected to the Friday release of the film version of his now-classic witch-hunt drama, ``The Crucible.'' By Jay Carr.Moved 12-16.  FILM-ANGELS-SFCHRON (Undated) _ Angels are coming to a theater near you, and they're anything but cherubic. John Travolta is the heavenly messenger in ``Michael,'' coming out on Christmas Day, while Denzel Washington appears as an angel named Dudley in ``The Preacher's Wife,'' which opened this weekend. Both tell the story of all-too-human angels who come down to earth and into the plots of romantic comedies. Both of these sexy saviors wear trench coats, but only Travolta has wings. By Don Lattin.Moved 12-12.  FILM-CLOONEY-SFCHRON (Beverly Hills) _ George Clooney arrived in Hollywood in the early 80s when the group of young actors known as the Brat Pack was on the rise. He read for the same parts as Judd Nelson and some of the others, but they were always hired instead. Then, in 1990, Clooney was almost cast as the hunky cowboy in ``Thelma &AMP; Louise.'' When Brad Pitt got the role, which would make him a star, ``I was pretty upset,'' Clooney admits. By Ruthe Stein.Moved 12-12.  BURTON-MOVIE-LADN (Los Angeles) _ He grew up on Evergreen Street in Burbank and discovered a playground for his imagination at the nearby Evergreen Cemetery, where he could take refuge from the world in the somber, spooky silences. In his new movie _ ``Mars Attacks!'' _ Tim Burton, now 38, indulges his childhood passion for B-grade space-invader movies with a goofy, extravagantly realized send-up of the genre. By Amy Dawes. Moved 12-12.  BACALL-MOVIES-NYT (Hollywood) _ She's 72, still glamorous and feisty, and looking for work. ``Suddenly I've finished three pictures, can you imagine?'' said Lauren Bacall with a soft laugh. ``But now I'm unemployed. I mean, I've had a lot of dry years. So these pictures will, I hope, bring attention to the fact that I am around, breathing, walking and talking at the same time.'' She shrugged. ``Whoever said it would be easy?'' By Bernard Weinraub. W/photo. Moved 12-18.  SHOBIZ-COW-BOS (Sherborn, Mass.) _ Last year, she was the Cow that Came for Christmas. Emily's true and touching tale is about to become, well, a moooovie. By Doreen Iudica Vigue. W/photo. Moved 12-17.  BEAVISGUY-AZR (Los Angeles) _ The dumber you are, the cooler you are. That's a concept Mike Judge has sold to the world's teenagers. When you meet Judge, you can't help but think that this is what his creations _ ``Beavis and Butt-head'' _ will look like if they ever grow up. Judge, who writes, draws and does the voices for both teenage masters of the vulgar, is balding, sallow and a solemn eye-averter. By Bob Fenster. Moved 12-18.  STARTREK-FRAKES-PROFILE-NYT (New York) _ Even out of his Starfleet uniform, Jonathan Frakes, who has made a lucrative career out of playing the clear-eyed executive officer of the starship Enterprise in the ``Star Trek'' spinoffs, looks like a man in orbit. And no wonder: After acting for more than 20 years, many of them during the 1980s playing what he calls ``villains of the week'' in television dramas like ``Quincy'' and ``Barnaby Jones,'' he has just made his debut as a film director in ``Star Trek: First Contact.'' By Michel Marriott. W/photo. Moved 12-17.  THEATER  ALLISON-JANNEY-THEATER-NYT (Undated) _ Noel Coward's ``Present Laughter'' may be the chain-smoking-est play on Broadway, and though Allison Janney maintains she had quit a half-pack-a-day habit, it looks like the actress has slid backward a bit. On Nov. 18, the day before her 36th birthday, Ms. Janney made her Broadway debut in the revival of the 1939 Coward comedy. She plays Liz, the former but not ex-wife of Garry Essendine (Frank Langella), a London matinee idol who heads a frenetic, neurotic show-biz family. By Jean Nathan. Moved 12-12. Photos, where indicated, have been sent to NYT photo clients. Graphics, where indicated, have been sent to NYT graphic clients. Nonsubscribers can make individual purchase by calling 212-556-4204.  For retransmission of these stories, call 212-556-1927.  WALL-ST-XMAS-PARTIES-NYT (New York) _ Oh, the joys of working on Wall Street. (This year.) After lean times, the Christmas party is back with verve for Wall Streeters, a reflection of a Dow Jones industrial average that has gone up more than 60 percent in the last two years, a market that has not been this strong in a two-year period since Dwight D. Eisenhower was president. Bonuses are back. But if the current holiday parties around this city _ at the Plaza Hotel, the St. Regis Hotel, the Knickerbocker Club and the Rainbow Room _ are brimming with the recovery they also exhibit a certain skittishness. By Monique P. Yazigi. W/photo. Moved 12-17.  FOREIGN-JOURNAL-BOS (Moscow) _ In most places, a two-day holiday is a win-win situation. You get the days off, and they come as part of a truncated work week. But in Russia things are never that easy. When the government pronounced last Thursday and Friday holidays in honor of the democratic constitution that Russia adopted in 1993, it quickly declared Saturday and yesterday ordinary weekdays, to make up for the lost productivity. Rather than a long weekend, Russian workers got a non-weekend. By David Filipov. Moved 12-16.  EARN-WHISTLE-BLOWERS-$ADV15-NYT (Undated) _ Sounding the trumpets for whistle-blowers. By Laura Mansnerus. Moved 12-12.  WAYS-OF-WALL-STREET-BLOOM (New York) _ Happy holidays to 3,700 BankAmerica Corp. workers. And to 6,000 employees of Siemens AG, the German electronics and engineering company. Not to mention 200 people at little Golden Books Family Entertainment Inc. Quite soon, most of you folks will be out of work. By David Pauly. Moved 12-12.  WALL-STREET-EMPLOYMENT-BLOOM (New York) _ Wall Street is providing a record number of jobs as booming financial markets and a surge in mergers and acquisitions led securities firms to go on a hiring binge, an industry group said. By Monique Wise and Scott Martin. Moved 12-12.  FANNIEMAE-VERDICT-NYT (Washington) _ A jury on Thursday awarded a former employee of the Federal National Mortgage Association $6.9 million for verbal harassment by a supervisor who the employee said subsequently reorganized her out of her job. Moved 12-12.  WELFARE-WORK-HNS (San Francisco) _ Nyisha Gleaves remembers clearly the day she realized she was smart enough. It was the day she walked into her boss's office, confident and sure, with a disk in hand of the work she had just completed _ a spreadsheet report that weeks before had seemed close to impossible. By Venise Wagner. Moved 12-12.  DOCUMENT-MANAGEMENT-BOS (Undated) _ The computer age may have cut down on the mountains of paper accumulating in many workplaces, but it created a whole new kind of electronic chaos. With more work being done on computers, many companies have lost control of their electronic files, often losing them in a labyrinth of diskettes and hard drives. And the disorganization threatens to worsen as technology gets more sophisticated and the Internet gains acceptance as a means to link far-flung workers. By Joann Muller.Moved 12-17.  COLUMNS  WORKING-WOMEN-KAN (Undated) _ Toys tell us a lot about society, a lot about what we think of the roles of men and women. No doubt, there are plenty of men who never owned dolls and are now great fathers, and plenty of women who once adored Easy-Bake Ovens and now have careers. But before you rush to fill your child's holiday wish list, take a minute to think about the messages you're sending. Do women really look like Barbie? Does software have to be violent? Do only girls like cooking and boys like building? By Jilian Mincer.Moved 12-18.  ATWORK-COLUMN-KAN (Undated) _ Oh, for a good office party...My company, like many, stopped financing holiday parties several years ago. Employees now get gift certificates for groceries instead, and most everybody understands the reasons. Many people didn't go to the parties, and it didn't seem fair to spend a lot of money for a ``benefit'' that didn't benefit everyone. There were worries about company liability if employees drank too much and got in wrecks on the way home. By Diane Stafford. Moved 12-12.  WORKING-COLUMN-HNS (Undated) _ In this season of Christmas specials, there are workplace lessons to be learned. By L.M. Sixel. Moved 12-12.  ONJOBS-COLUMN-BOS (Exeter, N.H.) _ The way Cletus Kijek tells it, Osram Sylvania has long allowed near-intolerable working conditions at its plant here. Kijek contends Osram avoided taking action by firing him last year _ days after he blew the whistle and contacted federal safety inspectors. As Osram Sylvania sees it, the company has maintained a safe workplace and has taken corrective action when needed. As for Kijek, company officials say: He was fired for excessive absenteeism and falsification of workers compensation claims. Who's right? By Diane E. Lewis. W/photo. Moved 12-17. COUNT-ENGLISHPATIENT-NYT (Budapest) _ The character compassionately depicted in the film ``The English Patient'' as a desert explorer with enigmatic origins is based on a real-life Hungarian who served German military intelligence in World War II and then apparently spied for the Soviet Union. Through an adventure-strewn life that traversed wildly different nations and cultures through two World Wars, the collapse of the Austro-Hungarian empire and the takeover of his country by the Communists, the tall, slender, slightly stooped, multilingual count often seemed many things to many people. Sam Smith of the Chicago Tribune wrote that Ron Artest is the culprit. According to the Trib, it seems Artest was tired of listening to Jordan talk trash, so he knocked him down and the two got in a fight. That's how the ribs were broken . . . Tinsley barely made it in the first round, which is a testament to his attitude as much as anything. Boston and Orlando, both looking for point guard help, didn't bother either. He eventually slid to 27, where the Hawks took him and then dealt him to the Pacers. "96620"	"we"	"hope"	" Cover story looks at plans for a revolutionary new generation of warships - a timely piece as America braces for battle in the Middle East. The vessels depicted are trimarans, ships with triple hulls that cut the water far more efficiently than single-hull designs - and are so stable that they operate in wild seas. These combatants range from the 1351/2-foot Blue Knight Patrol Boat to the battleship-size Leviathan. The ships will be packed with missiles and advanced electronic-warfare equipment, and the Leviathan sports ``rail guns,'' which will use electromagnetism to accelerate and hurl a torrent of rounds upon the enemy. Both the United States and Britain are intensively testing the trimaran design now; the ships won't be operational for at least a decade - and maybe, we hope, they won't be needed by then. Going into winter takes confidence, even in a normal year, even if it's nothing more than confidence in one's own preparations. Somehow that's not good enough this year. Like everyone, I find myself wanting the world to be right with itself again, even if only in the wrong old ways. In the heart of the reluctance I feel and that I hear in the voices of my neighbors, there's a longing for the inconsequential summer we were having not so many weeks ago. Longing is probably too strong a word. Better to say that the memory of what was, for many Americans, an uneventful August exerts a certain attraction right now. But the present is irrefutable. The leaves won't rise again, except on a cold wind. The NYT frontpage advisory, with layout description, will move by 7:30 p.m. ET. The night supervisor for The New York Times News Service is Mary Armstrong; phone: 888-346-9867 or 212-556-1927; e-mail: maryanytimes.com.  TERROR-INTEL (Washington) -- Dismissing reports of dissension within the administration, Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld said Tuesday that he supports President Bush's evolving position on overhauling the intelligence bureaucracy, and that suggestions to the contrary are nonsense. Needless to say, I'm a part of this administration, Rumsfeld said at an afternoon Pentagon briefing. I favor reform in the intelligence community, as the president does. By Philip Shenon and Thom Shanker.  VIOXX-ULCERS (Undated) -- Additional scrutiny on the side effects of Merck's arthritis and pain drug Vioxx. By Barry Meier.  CBS-RATHER (New York) -- Less than two months after acknowledging that he could not authenticate documents central to a broadcast report that raised fresh questions about President Bush's National Guard service, Dan Rather has decided to step down as anchor and managing editor of the CBS Evening News on March 9, on what will be his 24th anniversary behind the anchor desk. By Jacques Steinberg and Bill Carter.  With photos, NYT5-8.  An early lede has moved.  DEPORT-CHILDREN (New York) -- In April of last year, when her mother dropped by immigration headquarters to complete some paperwork, Virginia Feliz became part of a growing tribe of American children who have lost a parent to deportation. Her mother, Berly, 47, migrated here illegally a decade ago. She was quickly clapped into handcuffs, and within hours placed on a plane to her native Honduras, unable to say goodbye to her husband and little girl, both U.S. citizens. I'm not happy, I'm sad, said Virginia, now eight. Because it's not fair that everybody else has their mom except me. No one keeps track of exactly how many American children were left behind by the record 186,000 non-citizens expelled from the U.S. last year, or the 887,000 more required to make a voluntary departure. But immigration experts say there are tens of thousands of children every year who lose a parent to deportation. As the debate over immigration policy heats up, such broken families are troubling people on all sides, and challenging schools and mental health clinics in immigrant neighborhoods. By Nina Bernstein.  With photo, NYT25.  UKRAINE-RUSSIA (Moscow) -- Not since NATO's war in Kosovo, or perhaps the Cold War itself, have the political differences between Russia and the West appeared so starkly as they have in Ukraine's disputed presidential election. It is not just that Russia and President Vladimir V. Putin himself have come out so strongly for the candidate promising closer relations with Moscow, Viktor F. Yanukovich, while Europe and the United States are supporting his opponent, Viktor A. Yushchenko, albeit more subtly. It is that both sides -- rivals in what the United Financial Group in Moscow called on Tuesday a Cold-War-style proxy confrontation -- have staked out diametrically opposed views of what unfolded in Ukraine. A news analysis by Steven Lee Myers.  IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- Developments. Five-thousand Marines, British troops and Iraqi commandos launched raids and arrested suspected insurgents Tuesday in a new offensive aimed at clearing a swath of rebel hotbeds south of Baghdad, the American military said, calling it the next stage since the retaking of Fallujah to stop the Iraqi insurgency. In other violence, masked gunmen assassinated a Sunni cleric north of Baghdad -- the second such killing in two days -- and insurgents hit a U.S. convoy with a roadside bomb near the central Iraq city of Samarra, prompting the Americans to open fire, killing an Iraqi, hospital officials said. QUESTIONS, SUGGESTIONS: Call us at (212) 556-1518 or zap an e-mail message.  How many NYTRNG papers ran the photo by AP Photographer John Moore in last Wednesday's papers? I'd really like to know.  The image, which I bet is nominated for a Pulitzer, shows an Army nurse as he tries to save the life of an American soldier by giving him CPR as the soldier is being transported into a military hospital in Baghdad, Iraq on Tuesday, Nov. 9, 2004. The soldier died. The soldier was fatally wounded in a Baghdad firefight with insurgents. He lies on a stretcher and a wedding band is on his left hand that tells you he has a family. If you did run it then you know what I am talking about but the file should still be in your AP Server JBM107. I have archived it.  After being here for 20 years I can't ever remember running an image like this so big on 1A. When I found it in the AP server last Tuesday I made a proof and took it to Editor Skip Perez expecting to have a battle to get any ink on 1A. Skip agreed that the image was extremely powerful and illustrated the effects of war on our troops. We ran it 6 columns above the fold. Lots of other papers -- including the Tampa Tribune -- ran it about the same size.  The calls and the letters started coming in, about 20 calls the best I can figure. We have had multiple Letters to the Editor about the image. At first it was ugly, how dare: The Ledger show troops in this manner? How would you like it if it was your son? Then we started getting some positive letters. Skip Perez responded to some of them and his comments are really help to explain things to the readers.  Ledger Publisher John Fitzwater passed along your e-mail critical of our front-page photo showing the dying soldier in Iraq. I respect your point of view.  I don't profess to always be right or certain about my judgments. I have been doing this job for 28 years and have made my share of mistakes.I do know a powerful, heart-grabbing photo when I see one. This was such a rare photo. That is why we used it and played it as we did.  I have also been around this business long enough to know that it's risky for anyone to make judgments about how someone's family might feel about us publishing a photo of a family member in crisis or death. I have spoken to family members about tragic photos we've published and some have thanked me.  We received about 20 phone calls or letters concerning the photo. Most were critical, but if you've been following our letters column, many praised our decision as well. Attached is a copy of one letter, from a military veteran, whose view differs from yours.  Again, thank you for being a longtime subscriber of The Ledger. We value your loyalty. Most days, I hope, you enjoy and are illuminated by what you see and read.  My name is John Carter.I'm retired U.S. Army.I was a first sergeant. I want to say that picture that I saw was not inappropriate. That picture represents what really happens in war. People must be aware that the cost of freedom is so soldiers and marines that are dying. So thank you. You'll get some flack over this, but I support you one hundred percent.  We are close to making a decision on the new shooter who will be added to the staff in January of 05. I can tell you this, it will be a hire from inside the group and that shooter will be fully equipped with Canon EOS gear. The shooter will have to stay here 3 years to take ownership of the gear but his/her camera allowance for $3400 will also be given in 05.  This was the first hire for me in 14 years. We added stringers David Mills and Rick Runion to the staff then ... I am a little out of date. I did find one or two other people that are well worth hiring, I was real pleased with their abilities and attitude. The last shooter to leave the paper was Chris O'Meara 18 years ago.  Thanks to Doug Finger in Gainesville for nailing a nice image of a bank robber from Lakeland bent over the back of a cop car being arrested. The young man was from Lakeland and Doug got that into the caption so we picked it up.  We used lots of photos from the Florida - Florida State game in Sunday's paper. Photos from Ocala and Gainesville.  From what I can see, the RBGAN Greg Wolf took to Iraq is working fine. Wolf has posted some fine images with interest beyond the fact that N.C. troops are shown. A husband shot his wife and took the three children, then shot himself in a van. The children are safe but local cops are keeping us 2 miles from the site. I guess its time for a sit down with our new sheriff. AP is all over us for the pixs so you might see the images there and on Wieck ... More to come. First of all, I want to publicly thank Jamie Moncrief for all the help he has given me over the years; more help/encouragement than he realizes, I am sure.  And there's probably not a better teller of photo war stories than him, which made photo conferences all the more fun for everyone attending. I wish him all the best and look forward to the day he can return.  Photo-wise, we're doing the usual after-Thanksgiving shopping photos and gearing up for the Christmas season of choirs, nativity scenes, Santa, etc.  I'm working on the completion of an easy but interesting photo package about odd mailboxes in the area. I gave the local postal carriers a form to take with them on their routes that they could fill out when they came across mailboxes/posts they thought were clever, or just plain bizarre.  The response was about 15 mailboxes/posts of which about 10 were stand-outs, and along with a couple of others I had seen myself it should be a full page when said and done.  As much as I have grown cold toward Nikon equipment, I do have to say a good thing about the strength of the gear after enduring a massive rain storm during high school football games the week before last. It was the most torrential downpours I have ever had to shoot photos in except for a full-fledged hurricane 19 years ago.  During two games on the same Friday night I used, at different times, a lens rain cover, a courtroom sound-proofing body cover, towels draped over everything and sweatshirts ... anything dry I could find. Still, the gear was eventually soaked (including the front lens element of my 300mm), but the gear worked famously and shows no signs of damage from the moisture. As much as I have grown cold toward Nikon equipment, I do have to say a good thing about the strength of the gear after enduring a massive rain storm during high school football games the week before last. It was the most torrential downpours I have ever had to shoot photos in except for a full-fledged hurricane 19 years ago.  During two games on the same Friday night I used, at different times, a lens rain cover, a courtroom sound-proofing body cover, towels draped over everything and sweatshirts ... anything dry I could find. Still, the gear was eventually soaked (including the front lens element of my 300mm), but the gear worked famously and shows no signs of damage from the moisture. Times Regional Newspaper Group. For use by member papers only.  We have news from Lakeland, Ocala, Sarasota and Wilmington.  The goal of PHOTO-BUDGET is to be a forum for exchanging advice, equipment reviews and news about your photo department. We certainly want this weekly missive to reach photo editors, but any other shooter who sends his e-mail address to nytrengnytimes.com will be added to the list.  QUESTIONS, SUGGESTIONS: Call us at (212) 556-1518 or zap an e-mail message.  Part of this is repeated from last Tuesday's budget due to the Holiday week. I feel the subject is important and deserves more discussion. Some editors did respond, thanks.  How many NYTRNG papers ran the photo by AP Photographer John Moore in last Wednesday's papers? The image, which I bet is nominated for a Pulitzer, shows an Army Nurse as he tries to save the life of an American soldier by giving him CPR upon arrival at a military hospital in Baghdad, Iraq on Tuesday, Nov. 9, 2004. But the soldier died. The soldier was fatally wounded in a Baghdad firefight with insurgents. He lies on a stretcher and a wedding band is on his left hand that tells you he has a family. If you did run it then you know what I am talking about but the file should still be in your AP Server JBM107. I have archived it.  After being here for 20 years I can't ever remember running an image like this so big on 1A. When I found it in the AP server last Tuesday I made a proof and took it to Editor Skip Perez expecting to have a battle to get any ink on 1A. Skip agreed that the image was extremely powerful and illustrated the effects of war on our troops. We ran it six columns above the fold. Lots of other papers ran it about the same size, like the Tampa Tribune. The calls and the letters started coming in, about 20 calls the best I can figure. We have had Letters to the Editor about the image. At first it was ugly, how dare The Ledger show troops in this manner. How would you like it if it was your son? Then we started getting some positive letters. Skip Perez responded to some of them and his comments really help to explain things to the readers. The following is one of Skip's responses to a reader:  Dear Mr. Williams,  Ledger Publisher John Fitzwater passed along your e-mail critical of our front-page photo showing the dying soldier in Iraq. I respect your point of view.  I don't profess to always be right or certain about my judgments. I have been doing this job for 28 years and have made my share of mistakes. I do know a powerful, heart-grabbing photo when I see one. This was such a rare photo. That is why we used it and played it as we did.  I have also been around this business long enough to know that it's risky for anyone to make judgments about how someone's family might feel about us publishing a photo of a family member in crisis or death. I have spoken to family members about tragic photos we've published and some have thanked me.  We received about 20 phone calls or letters concerning the photo. Most were critical, but if you've been following our letters column, many praised our decision as well. Attached is a copy of one letter, from a military veteran, whose view differs from yours.  Again, thank you for being a longtime subscriber of The Ledger. We value your loyalty. Most days, I hope, you enjoy and are illuminated by what you see and read.  My name is John Carter. I'm retired U.S. Army. I was a first sergeant. I want to say that picture that I saw was not inappropriate. That picture represents what really happens in war. People must be aware that the cost of freedom is so soldiers and marines that are dying. So thank you. You'll get some flack over this, but I support you one hundred percent.  Responses from NYTRNG editors re. the photo:  We opted not to run the CPR photo.....being so close three area military bases, we thought it was a smattering too close to home for many family members. On Sunday the Tampa Bay Bucs and their worthless place kicker will play the Atlanta Falcons at Raymond James Stadium at 1PM. Photographer David Mills and one TBA will cover for deadline. All images will be posted to Wieck by 5PM.  The Herald-Tribune in Sarasota last month revived and expanded a photo feature called Life Through a Lens.  We have kicked around the idea for a photo column for quite a while and we finally made it happen, said MIKE LANG, director of photography.  A photo column, like a written column, has a point of view. While visual, photo columns usually include a narrative that pairs with images to express an idea. A photo column can have one image or several and a general goal is to convey an impression that readers haven't seen before.  Life Through a Lens is the name of a photo column that had run in one edition of the paper, but the new version runs prominently in all editions. It began its new life on Oct. 25 with a five-column spread of photos and text by CHIP LITHERLAND. Litherland captured the images after he finished an assignment at Mote Marine Laboratory in Sarasota and decided to play tourist for an hour.  Litherland wrote that Mote's aquariums provided an opportunity for instant nature photography but he did not take a conventional approach. His choice of black and white images added a sense of mystery to photos that include the fin of a tiger shark cutting through water, a moray eel showing his teeth, the silvery face of a lookdown and X-ray-like images of moon jellyfish.  The feature appears on the cover of the Florida West section every other Monday.  Since the relaunch, Life through a Lens has appeared three more times: THOMAS BENDER visited a group of Civil War re-enactors; PHIL DIEDERICH showed why he was sure a long election season was over; and Litherland looked for and found something different at a groundbreaking ceremony.  Space availability can vary greatly, so we will need to be flexible with the design, Lang said. One week, it might be several photos and a copy block written by the photographer. Another week, it might be a single image with a healthy cutline. Either way, it will be a great showcase for our staff photographers to publish photos that might not get in the paper anywhere else. , he adds,	 You're trying to produce pork at the lowest cost possible, says John Mabry, director of the Iowa Pork Industry Center at Iowa State University, and still have an acceptable product. It's cheaper to feed leaner pork, he adds, and therefore the price of commercial pork at the supermarket can be lower. John Lawrence, livestock economist at Iowa State, agrees, saying that 50 to 52 pounds of pork per person are consumed annually in American homes, and compared to beef and poultry, the commercial product is affordable. But the system has sacrificed taste. Pork went from being fatty to becoming extremely lean (the other white meat ad campaign was intended to counteract the popularity of chicken). As far back as 1977, James Beard wrote in his book Theory &amp; Practice of Good Cooking that lean pork was less flavorful and that he hoped the streamlined pork would soon fall out of fashion. </P> 	nyt_eng_200412.gz
, he says,	 More than 20 years later, Beard's notion has finally found supporters. John Dewar, who sells fine meats to chefs and in retail shops in Newton and Wellesley, thinks the pendulum has swung. Pork in earlier years was too fatty and greasy, Dewar says. Producers tried to counter by growing a slimmer pig, and, by god, they did. The result was dry and tasteless. The quick fix, he adds, was to inject the lean product with a solution to put some moisture into it. Although that might work at a retail level, he says, restaurant chefs weren't satisfied. </P> 	nyt_eng_200412.gz

CIA-LEAK-TIME (New York) -- Time magazine said Thursday that it would provide documents concerning the confidential sources of one of its reporters to a grand jury investigating the disclosure of the identity of a covert CIA agent, Valerie Plame. The U.S. Supreme Court turned down appeals in the case on Monday, concluding the gravest legal confrontation between the press and the government in a generation. Two reporters, Judith Miller of The New York Times and Matthew Cooper of Time magazine, face jail for refusing to testify before the grand jury. By Adam Liptak.  With a graphic.  An early lede has moved.  IRAN-HOSTAGES (Tehran, Iran) -- President-elect Mahmoud Ahmadinehad's possible role in the 1980 seizure of the American Embassy in Tehran escalated suddenly into a new complication on Thursday in the estranged relations between the United States and Iran, with the Bush administration saying it took seriously the accounts of several hostages who claimed Ahmadinedad was one of the leaders in the seizure. In Iran, at least two former leaders dismissed those accounts as ridiculous and one even said the president-elect had wanted to participate in the seizure but they refused to let him. By Nazila Fathi.  BOA-MBNA (New York) -- In a move to grab a bigger piece of consumers' wallets by becoming a powerhouse in credit cards, Bank of America announced plans on Thursday to acquire the card giant MBNA in a $35 billion cash and stock deal. The deal signals Bank of America's continued strategic bet on the consumer despite concerns that climbing interest rates are decelerating growth in critical areas, including home loans and even credit cards. Already the nation's biggest bank in deposits, Bank of America would leapfrog Citigroup and J.P. Morgan Chase to become the country's largest credit-card issuer with 20 percent market share. By Eric Dash.  BOA-MBNA-DEALMAKERS (New York) -- Executives involved in the negotiations help us reconstruct the behind-the-scenes machinations that led to MBNA's $35 billion deal with Bank of America announced on Thursday. Part of the drama was the crash into New York's East River of a helicopter carrying MBNA executives returning to Delaware from negotiations with Wachovia. Despite several harrowing moments trapped underwater in the helicopter, the executives managed to escape. The crash received extensive news coverage. But until Thursday, no one knew exactly why the executives were in Manhattan. By Andrew Ross Sorkin.  NY-ATTACK (New York) -- Two more white men were taken into custody on Thursday in an attack on a black man in Howard Beach, Queens, as Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg and prosecutors pledged to seek maximum penalties in the Wednesday beating. The two suspects who turned themselves in were accused of being among the three white men who confronted the victim, Glen Moore, in Howard Beach around 3 a.m. Wednesday. A third suspect, Nicholas Minucci, 19, was charged with first-degree assault as a hate crime in the attack. Moore, 20, was in critical but stable condition at Jamaica Hospital and was said to have lost hearing. By Jim Rutenberg.  With photo.  EBBERS-RESTITUTION (New York) -- Bernard J. Ebbers, the founder and former chief executive of WorldCom who was found guilty of fraud by a New York jury in March, has agreed to surrender nearly all of his approximately $40 million personal fortune to investors who lost billions when the telecommunications company spiraled into bankruptcy almost three years ago. MCI, the successor company to WorldCom, which emerged from bankruptcy protection in April 2004, will share in the restitution provided by the sale of Ebbers' assets, although the company stands to receive nowhere near the roughly $400 million it is owed by the former chief executive. By Gretchen Morgenson.  REPUBS-BLACKS (Clarion, Pa.) -- Lynn Swann, the Hall of Fame former wide receiver for the Pittsburgh Steelers, stood before a nearly all-white Republican crowd at the Holiday Inn here and blasted Pennsylvania's Democratic governor, Edward Rendell, for failing to reduce property taxes. Then, without prompting, Swann suddenly turned the subject to race, his own. I'm not here to be the poster child for the Republican Party, to say they're being inclusive by running an African American, said Swann, 53. That's not why I'm here. I'm here to win. But to many prominent Republicans, Swann, a commentator for ABC Sports is much more than a telegenic and potentially potent candidate for governor in 2006. He still has to get through a Republican primary. But he is, they hope, part of a bumper crop of prominent black candidates who will help the Republicans crack, if not break, Democratic domination among black voters in several important states over the coming year. By James Dao.  (Editors: Budgets and advisories are internal documents not for publication or redistribution outside of client news organizations. The odds of having a big income but not paying taxes on it are growing. About one in every 436 high-income Americans lived tax free in 2002, up from one in 531 in 2001 and one in 1,010 in 2000. By David Cay Johnson.  CO2-OCEANS (Undated) -- Carbon dioxide, regardless of whether it is contributing one whit to global warming, is turning the oceans acidic, the Royal Society in Britain warns. The acidity could dissolve and kill coral reefs and disrupt other marine life by the end of the century, the scientists said. Unlike the debate over global warming, where forecasts of rising temperatures are based on complex and incomplete computer models, the chemistry of carbon dioxide and sea water are simple and straightforward. By Kenneth Chang. Wellfleet and Truro have strong arts traditions of their own. But these towns have also benefited from some of Provincetown's modern problems. Skyrocketing real estate prices, dense summer crowds, and traffic -- at times, the roads in town resemble I-95 more than a bucolic byway, and locals talk about refusing to drive anywhere for fear of losing their parking space -- can make solitude, low-rent artists' studios, and funky writers' hovels as tough to find as local shellfish during a red tide. ON THE COVER  ENERGY-TRADING (Houston) -- The industry that Enron made infamous -- energy trading -- is springing to life again. Volatile energy markets and record-high commodity prices are prompting renewed interest from investors eager to play in the sector. That has pushed banks and a growing number of hedge funds to hire more energy traders and brainy quantitative minds to back their bets on energy prices. In Houston, New York and London, a scramble for top trading talent has ensued that rivals the cutthroat hiring frenzy of the late 1990s. By Alexei Barrionuevo.  MARKET-WATCH (Undated) -- Weekly Wall Street commentary. By Gretchen Morgenson.  ECON-COLUMN (Undated) -- Adding up the real cost of the Iraq war. By Louis Uchitelle.  EVERYBODYS-BUSINESS (Undated) -- None of us can predict or read the future. But we can read the past, and we can learn from it. By Ben Stein.  CONTRARY-COLUMN (Undated) -- With hundreds of cable channels on television and a seemingly infinite number of choices on the Internet, it's easy to assume that theaters, concert halls and ballparks must be chronically empty. Instead, the opposite is true. Wherever you look, it seems, people want to see something in person. Technology will never replace live performances. By Daniel Akst.  STRATEGIES-COLUMN (Undated) -- In 2002, Morningstar completed a major overhaul of its mutual fund rating system, significantly narrowing the categories in which individual funds were placed and evaluated. Enough time has elapsed to allow a meaningful test of how well the new system works as a tool for fund investors. The results are in -- and they are quite positive. By Mark Hulbert.  INVESTING  INVEST-SHAREHOLDERS (Undated) -- In the last year, hedge funds and deep-pocketed individual investors have been cajoling management at many large companies to buy back shares, increase dividends or take other steps to bolster shareholder value. For the most part, mutual funds -- the investment vehicle of choice for most investors -- have historically avoided shareholder activism, and most still do. But a handful have joined hedge funds and other investors on the corporate barricades. By Daniel Gross.  FEATURE  VIDEO-IPOD (Undated) -- Partly because of the popularity of Apple's new video iPod, introduced in October, the sight of people staring at Lost or other shows on tiny screens as they ride the bus may become as commonplace as that of people talking on cellphones. By Anne Eisenberg.  AUTO-SHOW (Detroit) -- While luxury automakers introduced titillating high-performance cars last week at the North American International Auto Show, it was mostly a reality show for real people who need real transportation. By Cheryl Jensen.  AUTO-DESIGN (Detroit) -- Big hips are back. The swollen rear fenders that dominated car design in the late 1960s, the shape of oversize muscle and latent sensuality, are bulging from many of the dream machines at the North American International Auto Show. By Phil Patton. Volatile energy markets and record-high commodity prices are prompting renewed interest from investors eager to play in the sector. That has pushed banks and a growing number of hedge funds to hire more energy traders and brainy quantitative minds to back their bets on energy prices. In Houston, New York and London, a scramble for top trading talent has ensued that rivals the cutthroat hiring frenzy of the late 1990s. By Alexei Barrionuevo.  With photos . But a handful have joined hedge funds and other investors on the corporate barricades. By Daniel Gross. TOP  Lede story (1-col. hed):  STEM-CELL (Undated) -- Biologists have developed a technique for establishing colonies of human embryonic stem cells from an early human embryo without killing it. This method, if confirmed in other laboratories, would seem to remove the principal objection to the research -- that it involves destruction of a human embryo. There is no rational reason left to oppose this research, said Dr. Robert Lanza, vice president of Advanced Cell Technology and leader of the team that is reporting the new method in the journal Nature. But critics of human embryonic stem cell research raised other objections, such as the possible risk to the embryo and the in vitro fertilization procedure itself, in which embryos are generated from a couple's egg and sperm. By Nicholas Wade.  IRAN-INTEL (Washington) -- Looking at the role of assessments by American intelligence about Iran in the formulation of administration policy. By Mark Mazzetti.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in i news file.)  Top left (4-col. photo, story at fold):  AFRICA-CHILD-LABOR (Lusaka, Zambia) -- The boulders here are hard enough that the scavengers who have taken over the abandoned quarry south of downtown prefer not to strike them directly with their hammers. They heat the rocks first -- with flaming tires, scrap plastic, even old rubber boots. At dusk, when three or four blazes spew choking black clouds across the huge pit, the quarry looks like a woodcut out of Dante. Alone Banda works in this purgatory six days a week. Nine years old, nearly lost in a hooded sweatshirt, he takes football-size chunks of fractured rock and beats them into powder with a thick steel bolt. In a good week, he says, he can make enough powder to fill half a cement bag. His grandmother sells the bag for about $3. Often, she said, it is the difference between eating and going hungry. Across most of the globe, the number of children forced to work is in sharp decline. But sub-Saharan Africa is the exception, in places like Lusaka and for children like Alone. Here, more than one in four children below age 14 works. By Michael Wines.  MIDEAST-GAZA (Jerusalem) -- An unknown Islamic group issued a sweeping demand on Wednesday for the release of all Muslim prisoners held in the United States in exchange for two Fox News television journalists seized at gunpoint last week in Gaza City. The group, calling itself the Holy Jihad Brigades, had not claimed responsibility or made any demands until Wednesday, when it released a statement along with a video of the two men, Steve Centanni, 60, an American based in Washington, and Olaf Wiig, 36, a cameraman from New Zealand. By Greg Myre.  KATRINA-EVACUEES (Atlanta) -- Kenneth Woodfin, who with his family is running a popular New Orleans-style sno-ball stand in Atlanta, is leading a more stable, prosperous life here than in the city he fled. And among the thousands of Katrina evacuees who went to Atlanta, the Woodfins are not an anomaly. Many went there by choice and have put down roots, embracing what they say is a higher standard of living and melting into thriving neighborhoods almost without a trace. But for those New Orleanians who went west instead of east, especially those borne by fate rather than preference, exile has been a very different experience. In Houston, it can seem as if the storm never ended. Many evacuees remain in restless limbo, unable to return home but alienated from their new city. No matter where they ended up, of course, evacuees are not at peace over the destruction of their homes and communities. But the divergent experiences of those who went to Houston and Atlanta suggest that recovery depends on more than individual resources and demographics. Just as important are less quantifiable factors: a sense of welcome and connection, the presence of friends and family, even how close the victim came to death during the disaster. By Shaila Dewan.  NY-SPITZER (New York) -- Fresh off a decisive victory, Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg started his second term this year with huge ambitions for economic development in the city. He wanted to restart the troubled rebuilding effort at ground zero, for example, and reshape the far West Side of Manhattan. In recent weeks, however, the administration has been finding its efforts stymied, at least in part because of a man who has little if any official say in these matters. Eliot Spitzer has not yet won the Democratic primary for governor, but already he has started flexing his political muscle, lobbying to put the brakes on projects that Bloomberg has been trying to accelerate. By Diane Cardwell and Charles V. Bagli.  MIDEAST-LEBANON (Beirut, Lebanon) -- Amnesty International accused Israel on Wednesday of war crimes in its monthlong battle with Hezbollah, saying Israel's bombing campaign amounted to indiscriminate attacks on Lebanon's civilian infrastructure and population. By John Kifner.  (Story has moved in i news file.)  MIDEAST-FORCE (Paris) -- France told Israel on Wednesday that it would increase its commitment of troops to a multinational peacekeeping force in southern Lebanon in the coming days. ECON-REPORT (Undated) -- In the latest and strongest indication that the frenzy of buying and selling homes is over, the National Association of Realtors reported on Wednesday that sales of previously owned homes in July fell to the lowest level in over two years. Prices flattened, and sellers waited longer and longer to find buyers for their homes. By Jeremy W. Peters.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in f news file.)  AUTO-INSURE (Undated) -- For the first time in decades, prices for car insurance are falling in New Jersey and insurance companies are fighting for drivers' business. The desk supervisor for The New York Times News Service is Mitch Keller, followed by Ray Krueger; phone: 888-346-9867 or 212-556-1927; e-mail: mikell@nytimes.com or krueger@nytimes.com.  ELECT-RDP (Alexandria, Va.) -- Democrats gained control of the Senate on Thursday when the Republican incumbent in Virginia, George Allen, conceded to Jim Webb, his Democratic challenger, completing a broad realignment of power in Washington. By Adam Nagourney and John M. Broder.  IRAQ-POLICY (Washington) -- In nominating Robert M. Gates as defense secretary, President Bush is trying to transform the debate over Iraq by putting the Defense Department in the hands of a man who has been sharply critical of the Pentagon leadership, senior administration officials said on Thursday. Gates has hinted to associates that he intends to bring in Pentagon advisers who have largely been frozen out of the Bush White House, the officials said. By David E. Sanger.  SENATE-DEMS-REID (Washington) -- A Man in the News profile of Sen. Harry Reid. By Mark Leibovich.  BUSH-FUTURE (Washington) -- Can President Bush reinvent himself? Bush came to Washington with a record of working with moderate Democrats in Texas and a promise to be a uniter, not a divider. But for nearly six years, his uniting has consisted mostly of uniting his Republican base. Now, with two years left in his presidency, his last election behind him and a political hurricane having put Democrats in charge on Capitol Hill, Bush says he wants to find common ground across the aisle. By Sheryl Gay Stolberg.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in w news file.)  CONGRESS-DEMS (Washington) -- Little more than a year after Sen. Harry Reid predicted it would take a miracle for Democrats to seize the Senate, the presumptive new majority leader held a victory rally on Thursday afternoon to celebrate the fact that something like a miracle had occurred with the Democrats' winning six seats -- the final one coming with Sen. George Allen's concession an hour earlier. Democrats can now claim complete control of Congress, a stunning takeover that provides the party with new power over President Bush's nominations and foreign policy agenda. By Carl Hulse.  IRAQ-BATTALION-LOSSES (Baghdad, Iraq) -- Memorial services honoring fallen soldiers from the 1st Battalion, 22nd Infantry Regiment in Iraq used to require planning meetings of as long as 45 minutes. But at this point, they take barely five. We're here again, said Chaplain John Hill. A roadside bomb had killed yet another soldier from the battalion the day before. The battalion lost nine comrades just as it was beginning to make preparations for coming home later this month. By Michael Luo and Michael Wilson.  OBIT-BRADLEY (Undated) -- Ed Bradley, a pioneering black journalist who was a fixture in American living rooms on Sunday nights for more than a quarter-century as a correspondent on 60 Minutes, died on Thursday. He was 65. By Jacques Steinberg.  MIDEAST (Beit Hanoun, Gaza Strip) -- Palestinians marched in anger and mourning on Thursday for 18 civilians killed by Israeli artillery, baring for cameras the battered faces of two dead children. Prime Minister Ehud Olmert of Israel offered to ease tensions by meeting the Palestinian president anytime, anyplace. I am very uncomfortable with this event, Olmert said at a business conference in Tel Aviv. I'm very distressed. By Ian Fisher.  (Story has moved in i news file.)  ADS-ENTERTAIN (Undated) -- Marketers have found a new way to try to keep viewers from tuning out: urge them to tune in by creating their own TV shows, movies and online programming -- often with help from advertising agencies. These new offerings, they hope, will be entertaining enough to endear viewers to the brands behind them. By Louise Story.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in f news file.)  OBIT-WOLF (Frankfurt, Germany) --Markus Wolf, the famously elusive spymaster of communist East Germany whose feats of espionage were the stuff of Cold War legend, died on Thursday. He was 83. By Mark Landler.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in a news file.)  (Editors: Budgets and advisories are internal documents not for publication or redistribution outside of client news organizations.
